Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
The Ruling and Nature of Sale by Mutual Delivery (Bay' al-Ta'ati)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether bilateral delivery is required in bay' al-ta'ati

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on bay' al-ta'ati, identify the three intra-Hanafi views on bilateral delivery, and apply the majority view.

Core Idea
Mutual consent is enough for sale; but is bilateral delivery required? The majority says yes.

This lesson dives into a key dispute within the Hanafi school: what exactly is needed for a valid sale by mutual delivery (ta'ati)? The source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines ta'ati, explains why it is valid based on consent, and then unpacks the linguistic and methodological reasons behind three different Hanafi views on whether both parties must physically hand over the goods and price. By the end, you'll be able to navigate this internal ikhtilaf and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, determine what act is being discussed: bay' al-ta'ati (sale by mutual delivery). State its ruling: it is valid (sahih) and binding (lazim).
2
Grasp the underlying principle
Understand that the Hanafi school considers the 'meaning' (ma'na) of transfer of ownership as the essential element in commutative contracts, not mere wording. This is why consent-based conduct suffices.
Contrast this with divorce and manumission, where wording is paramount.
3
Define ta'ati linguistically
Recognize that 'ta'ati' derives from mufa'ala, implying mutual action. This linguistic point is the basis for the majority view that both parties must give.
Linguistic derivation (talil) is a key argument type here.
4
List the three intra-Hanafi views
From the source, extract the three positions: (1) both must deliver (majority), (2) one side's delivery suffices (some from Imam Muhammad), (3) seller's delivery alone with price specification suffices (al-Kirmani).
Trace each back to its interpretation of Imam Muhammad's statements.
5
Apply the majority view
Identify the mufta bihi position (bilateral delivery) and see how it applies to real cases: e.g., buyer taking goods without paying triggers compulsion by the judge.
Also note exceptions like bread/meat where price need not be specified.

قوله : ( وبتعاط) أي ويلزم البيع بالتعاطي أيضاً لأن جوازه باعتبار الرضا وقد وجد. وقد بناه في الهداية على أن المعتبر في هذه العقود هو المعنى والإشارة إلى العقود التمليكية كما في المعراج فخرج الطلاق والعتاق فإن اللفظ فيهما يقام مقام المعنى قال: ولا يلزم على أصحابنا شركة المفاوضة فإنهم قالوا : إنها تنعقد بلفظ المفاوضة فقط لأن عقد المفاوضة لما توقف على شروط لا يهتدي إلى استيفائها العوام في معاملاتهم حتى لو كانا عالمين بشروطها فعقدوها بلفظ آخر مع استيفاء الشروط صح؛ كذا في شرح المجمع اهـ. وفي فتح القدير بعد نقل ما في المعراج وأنت تعلم أن إقامة اللفظ مقام المعنى أثر في ثبوت حكمه بلا نية ليس غير فإذا قارنت هذه العقود ذلك اقتضى أن لا يثبت بمجرد اللفظ بلا نية فلا يثبت بلفظ البيع حكمه إلا إذا أراده به، وحينئذ فلا فرق بين بعت وأبيع في توقف الانعقاد به علی النية، ولذا لا ينعقد بلفظ بعت هزلاً فلا معنى لقوله ينعقد بلفظ الماضي ولا ينعقد بلفظ المستقبل اهـ. وهذا سهو فإن المراد أن البيع لا يختص بلفظ وإنما يثبت الحكم إذا وجد معنى التمليك والتملك بخلاف الطلاق والعتاق فإنه لا يعتبر المعنى فيهما وإنما تعتبر الموضوعة لهما صريحاً كان أو كناية، ولذا قالوا لو قال لها طلقي نفسك نصف تطليقة فطلقت نفسها واحدة لم يقع وإن كان الطلاق لا يتجزى، وإذا قال لها طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً فطلقت عشراً لا يقع وإن كان الطلاق لا مزيد له على الثلاثة. ثم اعلم أن المعنى وإن كان معتبراً في البيع ونحوه خاصة لا بد من صحة الاستعارة إذا كان اللفظ مجازاً ولذا قالوا: لو قال بعتك هذا بغير ثمن كان باطلاً ولا يكون مجازاً عن الهبة مع أنه أتى بمعناها، وكذا لو قال أجرتك داري شهراً بغير شيء لا يكون عارية مع أنه أتى بمعناها، وكذا لو قال اشتريت منك خدمة عبدك هذا شهراً بكذا وكذا فهو إجارة فاسدة، وكذا لو قال بعت منك منافع الدار شهراً بكذا فهي إجارة فاسدة فلم تعتبر المعنى والمسائل في الخلاصة والخانية بخلاف ما إذا قال أعرتك داري شهراً بكذا فهي إجارة، وكذا وهبتك منافعها شهراً بكذا اعتباراً للمعنى. وحقيقة التعاطي وضع الثمن وأخذ المثمن عن تراض منهما من غير لفظ، وهو يفيد من المعاطاة مفاعلة أنه لا بد من الإعطاء من الجانبين لأنه من وهي فتقتضي حصولها من الجانبين كالمضاربة والمقاسمة والمخاصمة وعليه أكثر المشايخ كما ذكره الطرسوسي وأفتى به الحلواني . وفي البزازية أنه المختار، وصح في فتح القدير أن إعطاء أحدهما كافٍ، ونص محمد على أن بيع التعاطي يثبت بقبض أحد البدلين وهذا ينتظم المبيع والثمن. ونصه في الجامع على أن تسليم المبيع يكفي لا ينفي الآخر. واكتفى الكرماني بتسليم المبيع مع بيان الثمن، أما إذا دفع الثمن ولم يقبض المبيع لا يجوز لأن المبيع أصل إلا إذا كان بيع مقايضة ؛ كذا في البزازية . فقد تحرر أن في المسألة ثلاثة أقوال وفي القاموس : التعاطي التناول. وهكذا في الصحاح والمصباح. وهو إنما يقتضي الإعطاء من جانب والأخذ من جانب لا الإعطاء من الجانبين كما فهم الطرسوسي. وأصل الاختلاف إنما نشأ من كلام الإمام محمد فإنه ذكر بيع التعاطي في مواضع فصوره في موضع بالإعطاء من الجانبين ففهم البعض أنه شرط، وصوره في موضع بالإعطاء من أحدهما ففهم البعض بأنه يكتفي به وصوره في موضع بتسليم المبيع ففهم البعض على أن تسليم الثمن لا يكفي كما ذكره في الذخيرة وصورته أحدهما أن من يتفقا على الثمن ثم يأخذ المشتري المتاع ويذهب به برضا صاحبه من غير دفع الثمن أو يدفع الثمن المشتري للبائع ثم يذهب من غير تسليم المبيع فإن البيع لازم على الصحيح حتى لو امتنع أحدهما بعده أجبره القاضي. وهذا فيما ثمنه غير معلوم، أما الخبز واللحم فلا يحتاج فيه إلى بيان الثمن كما في البزازية. ومن بيع التعاطي حكماً ما إذا جاء المودع بأمة غير المودعة وقال هذه أمتك والمالك يعلم أنها ليست إياها وحلف فأخذها حل الوطء للمودع وكان بيعاً بالتعاطي وعن أبي يوسف : لو قال للخياط ليست هذه بطانتي فحلف الخياط أنها هي وسعه أخذها وينبغي تقييده فيما إذا كانت العين ملكاً للدافع، أما إذا لم تكن ملكاً له فلا. ومنه قول الدلال للبزاز إن هذا الثوب بدرهم فقال ضعه وكذا بكم تبيع قفيز حنطة.
His statement: (and by mutual delivery) meaning that sale by mutual delivery is also binding because its permissibility is based on mutual consent, and that has been found. And he (al-Marghinani) built it in al-Hidayah upon the principle that in these contracts what is considered is the meaning (intent), and the indication refers to contracts of transfer of ownership as in al-Mi'raj, thus divorce and manumission are excluded because in them the wording stands in place of the meaning. He said: and this does not necessitate for our scholars (the validity of) partnership of muwafada, for they said it is concluded only with the wording of muwafada, because the contract of muwafada depends on conditions that common people cannot fulfill in their transactions, such that if both were knowledgeable of its conditions and concluded it with another wording while fulfilling the conditions, it would be valid. Similarly in Sharh al-Majma'. And in Fath al-Qadir after quoting what is in al-Mi'raj: and you know that the substitution of wording for meaning has an effect in establishing its ruling without intention, nothing else. So when these contracts acquire that, it requires that they are not established by mere wording without intention, so the ruling of sale is not established by the wording 'bought' unless one intends it by it. Then there is no difference between 'bought' (past tense) and 'I buy' (future tense) in that the conclusion depends on intention, therefore it is not concluded by the word 'bought' jokingly. Hence there is no meaning to the statement 'it is concluded with past tense and not with future tense.' This is a mistake, for the intended meaning is that sale is not restricted to a specific word; rather the ruling is established when the meaning of transfer of ownership (tamlik) and acquisition (tamalluk) is found, unlike divorce and manumission where the meaning is not considered, rather only the conventional usages are considered, whether explicit or implicit. So they said: if he tells her 'divorce yourself half a divorce' and she divorced herself one, it does not occur, even though divorce is indivisible; and if he says 'divorce yourself three' and she divorces ten, it does not occur, even though divorce is not more than three. Then know that meaning, though considered in sale and the like, necessarily requires the validity of isti'ara (metaphorical use) if the word is figurative, so they said: if one says 'I sold you this without a price' it is void and is not a metaphor for gift, even though he brought its meaning; similarly if he says 'I rented you my house for a month for nothing' it is not a loan, even though he brought its meaning; similarly if he says 'I bought from you the service of your slave for a month for such-and-such' it is a void lease; similarly if he says 'I sold you the usufruct of the house for a month for such-and-such' it is a void lease; thus the meaning was not considered. These cases are in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya. Unlike if he says 'I loaned you my house for a month for such-and-such' it is a lease, and similarly 'I gifted you its usufruct for a month for such-and-such' considering the meaning. The reality of mutual delivery is placing the price and taking the priced object with mutual consent without speech. And it implies from mu'ata (mutual interaction) that it must be from both sides because it is of the pattern mufa'ala, which requires occurrence from both sides like mudaraba, muqasama, and mukhasama. According to most mashayikh as mentioned by al-Tarsusi, and al-Halwani gave fatwa accordingly. In al-Bazzaziyya it is the chosen view. And in Fath al-Qadir it is correct that one side's giving is sufficient. And Muhammad explicitly stated that sale by mutual delivery is established by the exchange of one of the two counter-values, and this includes both commodity and price. And his text in al-Jami' states that delivery of the commodity suffices, not negating the other. And al-Kirmani sufficed with delivery of the commodity with specification of price. As for paying the price without receiving the commodity, it is not permissible because the commodity is the principal, unless it is barter. So it is established that there are three views on the matter. In al-Qamus: mutual delivery means taking. Similarly in al-Sihah and al-Misbah. And it only implies giving from one side and taking from the other, not giving from both sides as al-Tarsusi understood. The origin of the disagreement arose from Imam Muhammad's statements: he described sale by mutual delivery in some places as giving from both sides, so some understood that it is a condition; he described it in another place as giving from one side, so some understood that it suffices; he described it in yet another place as delivery of the commodity, so some understood that paying the price is not sufficient. As mentioned in al-Dhakhira, one of his descriptions is that two parties agree on a price, then the buyer takes the goods and goes away with the seller's consent without paying the price, or the buyer pays the price to the seller and then goes away without delivery of the goods; in either case the sale is binding, so much so that if one of them refuses thereafter, the judge compels him. This applies when the price is unknown. As for bread and meat, there is no need to specify the price, as in al-Bazzaziyya. And from the constructive sale by mutual delivery is the case when the depositor brings a slave-girl not belonging to the depositee and says 'this is your slave-girl' while the owner knows she is not his, and he swears and takes her; then intercourse becomes permissible for the depositee, and this is a sale by mutual delivery. And from Abu Yusuf: if he said to the tailor 'this is not my lining' and the tailor swore that it is, it is permissible for him to take it. This should be restricted to when the item is owned by the giver; if not owned by him, then not. And from it is the statement of the broker to the cloth merchant: 'this cloth is for a dirham' and he said 'put it,' and similarly 'for how much do you sell a bushel of wheat?'
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe into the ruling and nature of 'bay' al-ta'ati' (sale by mutual delivery) within the Hanafi school. The author begins by affirming that sale by ta'ati is binding because the element of consent (rida) is present, which is the fundamental pillar of contracts in the Hanafi school. He builds on the principle established in Al-Hidayah that in commutative contracts (such as sale, lease, gift) what matters is the underlying meaning (ma'na) of transfer of ownership, not the specific wording. This distinguishes them from divorce and manumission, where the verbal formula itself substitutes for meaning and no consideration of intent is needed after the conventional wording is pronounced. The passage then delves into a critical refinement of Fath al-Qadir, which questioned the distinction between past and future tense, but the author corrects it by clarifying that all these contracts depend on intention, but the requirement of a specific wording is not necessary; rather, any conduct indicating mutual consent suffices. The author then discusses the limits of considering meaning: even though meaning is primary, the figurative use (isti'ara) must be sound; otherwise, a meaningless expression like 'sold without a price' does not become a valid gift because the metaphorical transfer is invalid. The definition of ta'ati is then provided: physically handing over the price and taking the goods with mutual consent without verbal offer and acceptance. The term itself is derived from mufa'ala, implying bilateral action, and the majority of Hanafi scholars (like al-Tarsusi and al-Halwani) hold that both parties must give. However, there are three views within the school: (1) both sides must give (majority view), (2) one side giving suffices (upheld in Fath al-Qadir and attributed to Imam Muhammad), (3) delivery of the commodity alone suffices (al-Kirmani's view). The author traces this disagreement to Imam Muhammad's own varying statements in different contexts. Practical examples illustrate the binding nature: if the buyer takes goods without paying, the sale is binding and the judge compels payment. For necessities like bread and meat, price specification is not required. The passage also includes constructive cases: a depositor giving a non-owned slave-girl to the owner, who swears and takes her, results in a sale by ta'ati; and a tailor's dispute over lining fabric resolved by oath and acceptance. These examples show the breadth of the concept in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): The physical exchange of price and commodity without verbal offer and acceptance, based on mutual consent.
المعنى (al-ma'na): The underlying meaning or intention of the contract, such as transferring ownership, as opposed to the literal wording.
المضاربة (al-mudaraba): A partnership where one provides capital and the other labor, requiring bilateral action (mufa'ala).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, what is required for the validity of sale by mutual delivery (bay' al-ta'ati)?
A) Both parties must verbally state offer and acceptance.
B) Only one party needs to hand over the price or the goods.
C) Both parties must physically deliver the price and the goods to each other.
D) The seller must deliver the goods, but the buyer may pay later.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates sale by mutual delivery (ta'ati) based on consent, and the majority requires both parties to physically deliver the countervalues, supported by the linguistic derivation of the term.

Definition and Operation of Bai' al-Mu'atah
Defines bai' al-mu'atah and gives examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define bai' al-mu'atah and identify valid examples of such transactions.

Core Idea
Mu'atah is a valid sale by mutual giving without speech, binding upon delivery with consent.

The passage opens by clarifying that mere expression of meaning does not change contract type—then defines the reality of mu'atah: placing the price and taking the goods with mutual consent without verbal offer and acceptance. Understanding this definition is key to grasping the many examples that follow, from bread purchases to complex depository scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's method
Note how the author first shows that verbal formulas without proper terms (like 'I sold for nothing') do not create a valid gift or loan, establishing that contract types are determined by essential elements, not mere words.
This sets the stage: mu'atah is a genuine contract, not a verbal metaphor.
2
Extract the core definition
Find the sentence: 'The reality of mu'atah is placing the price and taking the priced item with mutual consent without speech.' Highlight the three conditions: actual delivery, mutual consent, and absence of speech.
The root w-h-y implies reciprocal taking, but the author will later dispute whether both sides must give.
3
Analyze the examples
Work through the examples: buyer takes goods without paying, buyer pays without taking goods (except barter), broker's transaction, depository of a slave-girl, tailor's lining, and the return of defective goods. For each, identify how mutual consent and delivery occur without words.
Note that for bread and meat, no price specification is needed; for other items, price may be known by agreement.
4
Note the boundary conditions
Identify that mu'atah after a void contract is not valid unless separated from the first contract, and that the command to set aside or weigh stands for delivery. Also note that mu'atah applies to both low-value and high-value items, contrary to some views.
This refines the definition: mu'atah is not limited to cheap goods.

This: He said, 'I sold you this for nothing' is void and is not a metaphor for gift even though he expressed its meaning. Similarly, if he said, 'I rented my house to you for a month for nothing' it is not a loan even though he expressed its meaning. Similarly, if he said, 'I bought from you the service of your slave for a month for such and such' it is a void lease. Similarly, if he said, 'I sold you the benefits of the house for a month for such and such' it is a void lease, so the meaning was not considered—the issues are in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya—contrary to when he said, 'I lend you my house for a month for such and such' it is a lease, and similarly, 'I gifted its benefits to you for a month for such and such' considering the meaning. The reality of mu'atah is placing the price and taking the priced item with mutual consent without speech. It indicates from the verbal noun mu'atah that giving from both sides is necessary because it is from the root w-h-y, so it requires occurrence from both sides, like mudarabah, muqasamah, and mukhasamah. This is the view of most scholars as mentioned by al-Tarsusi and al-Halwani gave fatwa accordingly. In al-Bazzaziyya it is the chosen view. It is established in Fath al-Qadir that giving from one side is sufficient. Muhammad stated that the sale of mu'atah is established by taking delivery of one of the two countervalues, and this covers the sold item and the price. His text in al-Jami' states that delivery of the sold item suffices, not negating the other. Al-Kirmani sufficed with delivery of the sold item with specification of the price. As for if he paid the price and did not take the sold item, it is not permissible because the sold item is the principal, except in case of barter. Thus it is established that there are three opinions on the issue. In al-Qamus: al-ta'ati is taking. Similarly in al-Sihah and al-Misbah. It only implies giving from one side and taking from the other, not giving from both sides as al-Tarsusi understood. The origin of the difference arose from the words of Imam Muhammad, for he mentioned bai' al-mu'atah in places: he portrayed it in one place as giving from both sides, so some understood it as a condition; he portrayed it in another place as giving from one side, so some understood that it suffices; he portrayed it in a place as delivery of the sold item, so some understood that delivery of the price does not suffice, as mentioned in al-Dhakhira. One of its forms: two people agree on the price, then the buyer takes the goods and goes away with the seller's consent without paying the price, or the buyer pays the price to the seller then goes away without delivery of the sold item—then the sale is binding correctly, so that if one of them later refuses, the judge compels him. This is when the price is unknown; as for bread and meat, there is no need to specify the price, as in al-Bazzaziyya. From the legal mu'atah: when the depositor comes with a slave-girl other than the deposited one and says 'This is your slave-girl' and the owner knows she is not hers and swears, and he takes her, then intercourse is permissible for the depositor, and it is a sale by mu'atah. From Abu Yusuf: if he said to the tailor, 'This is not my lining' and the tailor swore that it is, it is permissible for him to take it. It should be restricted to when the item is owned by the giver; if not owned by him, then no. From this is the saying of the broker to the cloth-seller: 'This cloth is for a dirham' and he said 'put it down'; similarly, 'For how much do you sell a qafiz of wheat?' if he is present—then he becomes an addressee by speech, and what was of speech in the first session does not remain to the second session; hence the witnesses in the second session hear only one of the two parts of the contract, and hearing the witness of one part of the contract is acceptance. Al-Khulasa: He said, 'For a dirham' and he said, 'Set it aside' and he set it aside, it is a sale. Similarly, if he said to the butcher similarly. From this: if he returned it due to option of defect and the seller is certain it is not his and he took it and was pleased, it is a sale by mu'atah as in Fath al-Qadir. Based on this, consent is necessary in the deposited slave-girl and the tailor's lining. Based on this, the command to set aside or weigh suffices for delivery, so this is a mu'atah sale and there is no delivery from either side because the command to set aside or weigh stands in place of delivery. The offer must be established because it necessitates the precedent of 'I bought' just as 'take' necessitates the precedent of sale, and the weighing of the addressee is acceptance because we said it occurs by action. So weighing and setting aside are actions; therefore it should not be included here as Ibn al-Humam did. We have stated in offer and acceptance that they, after a void contract, do not effect the sale before separation from the void contract, so in bai' al-mu'atah a fortiori. This is explicit in al-Bazzaziyya that mu'atah after a void or invalid contract does not effect the sale because it is based on the previous. This is interpreted as we mentioned, and it is absolute, including the low-value and high-value, because the meaning includes all, and this is the correct and relied-upon view as in al-Hidayah and others. In al-Hawi al-Qudsi: The well-known view is that it is not permissible for valuable items. I say: What he claimed as well-known contradicts the well-known. Al-Nafis is what is high in value like a slave, and al-khasis is what is low in value like bread. Some define al-nafis as the nisab of theft or more, and al-khasis as less than that. In al-Bazzaziyya: He bought a load for eight, then said, 'Bring another load and dump it here' and he did, then he demands the price. He said to the butcher, 'How much of this meat for a dirham?' He said, 'Two mans' and he gave the dirham and took it, it is a valid sale and he does not repeat the weighing. If he weighed and found it short, he reclaims the proportion from the dirham, not from the meat, because the contract was formed on the amount of the sold item. In one session is a condition for the validity of marriage. End. His statement: 'So in bai' al-mu'atah a fortiori.' I say: Mentioned in al-Nihaya, al-Kifaya, and Fath al-Qadir at the statement of al-Hidayah: 'And whoever sells a pile of food, each qafiz for a dirham, the sale is valid in one qafiz according to Abu Hanifa unless he specifies all its qafizes. And the two said it is valid in both ways.' End. For them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], the ignorance is in their hands to remove, and such ignorance does not prevent. But if it is said: Such ignorance does prevent as in sale by number, for it is void even though removing ignorance is in their hands, we say: The sale by number is void because it involves an increase of ignorance entrenched in the core of the contract—ignorance of the price due to a number unknown to the buyer, so it becomes like gambling because it is possible that the seller reveals the amount of the number as ten dirhams or more or less. Regarding this, Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani said: Even if he learns the number in the session, that contract does not become valid, but if the seller is continuously content and the buyer is pleased, a new contract is formed initially by mutual consent. In al-Fath he expressed it as mu'atah and sometimes as mutual consent, and mu'atah means the same. Then the restriction of the issue—namely the non-conclusion of sale by mu'atah after a void contract before separation—applies if that is after the session. As for if they mutually consent in it, it is concluded without separation from the first void contract, as is explicit in the statement of Shams al-A'imma, unless it is restricted to after separation from the first, so let it be considered. See what comes in the commentary on his statement 'and if he sold a flock or a garment' and perhaps there are two opinions on the issue. His statement: 'and it is interpreted as we mentioned' i.e., that the non-
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, commenting on the definition and conditions of bai' al-mu'atah (transaction by mutual giving). The author first clarifies that mere expression of meaning does not change the legal nature of a contract; for example, saying 'I sold you this for nothing' is void and not a gift, and saying 'I rented you my house for a month for nothing' is not a loan. Then he defines the reality of mu'atah: placing the price and taking the priced item with mutual consent without verbal offer and acceptance. The key dispute is whether mutual giving from both sides is required. Most scholars, including al-Tarsusi and al-Halwani, hold that it is necessary, based on the verbal noun pattern mu'afalah which implies reciprocal action. Others, like the author of Fath al-Qadir, consider giving from one side sufficient. This difference originates from Imam Muhammad's various statements: in one place he described mu'atah as giving from both sides, in another as one side, and in another as delivery of the sold item. The author discusses three opinions and then provides examples: taking goods with consent without payment, or paying without taking goods, constitute valid mu'atah. Further examples include depository scenarios and tailor's lining. The passage then addresses that mu'atah after a void contract is not valid unless separated from the first contract, and that mu'atah is valid for both low-value and high-value items, contrary to the claim in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Finally, a tangent discussion on bai' al-raqam (sale by unknown number) clarifies that it is void due to excessive ignorance, but if both parties consent in the session, a new contract can be formed by mu'atah.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): The definition given here is 'placing the price and taking the priced item with mutual consent without speech'. It is synonymous with mu'atah.
المعاطاة (al-mu'atah): From the root w-h-y, implying reciprocal giving; it is the actual transaction of giving and taking without verbal contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid bai' al-mu'atah according to the relied-upon Hanafi view?
A) A buyer says 'I buy this for a dirham' and the seller says 'I sell'.
B) A buyer takes a loaf of bread and leaves money on the counter without speech.
C) A seller says 'I sell you this for nothing' and hands it over.
D) A buyer pays the price but does not take the good, and then changes his mind.

Key Takeaway
Bai' al-mu'atah is a valid sale by mutual giving without speech, binding upon delivery with consent, and applies to all items regardless of value.

intermediate

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on 'Giving from Both Sides' in Bai' al-Mu'atah
Discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether mutual giving is required in mu'atah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi views on whether both parties must give in mu'atah and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Most Hanafis require giving from both sides, but the mufta bihi view suffices with one side.

The passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute about a key condition of mu'atah: must both the buyer and seller physically deliver their countervalues, or does delivery by one side suffice? This disagreement stems from Imam Muhammad's varied statements and has practical consequences for when a mu'atah sale is complete. Understanding the arguments and the tarjih is essential for applying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views from the passage: (1) Both sides must give (majority, al-Tarsusi, al-Halwani). (2) One side suffices (Fath al-Qadir, al-Bazzaziyya). (3) Delivery of the sold item with price specification suffices (al-Kirmani).
Note the authorities: Imam Muhammad's texts are the source of the ikhtilaf.
2
Examine the linguistic argument
The word mu'atah is on the pattern mu'afalah, which typically implies reciprocal action (like mudarabah). This leads the majority to require two-sided giving. However, the language of the passage also notes that the root w-h-y can simply mean 'taking' from one side.
This is a classic example of linguistic interpretation affecting fiqh.
3
Analyze Imam Muhammad's statements
The passage explains that Imam Muhammad described mu'atah in different ways: sometimes portraying two-sided giving, sometimes one-sided. These variations led different scholars to different conclusions. Understand that this is the origin of the dispute.
Imam Muhammad's status as a primary narrator makes his statements authoritative but not always unambiguous.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Look for the author's tarjih: he sides with the view that one side suffices, citing al-Bazzaziyya and Fath al-Qadir as the chosen (mukhtar) and relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The author also notes that the passage is interpreted to include all items, low and high value, reinforcing the chosen view.

Source Text

هذه قال بعتك هذا بغير ثمن كان باطلاً ولا يكون مجازاً عن الهبة مع أنه أتى بمعناها، وكذا لو قال أجرتك داري شهراً بغير شيء لا يكون عارية مع أنه أتى بمعناها، وكذا لو قال اشتريت منك خدمة عبدك هذا شهراً الاختلاف إنما نشأ من كلام الإمام محمد فإنه ذكر بيع التعاطي في مواضع فصوره في موضع بالإعطاء من الجانبين ففهم البعض أنه شرط، وصوره في موضع بالإعطاء من أحدهما ففهم البعض بأنه يكتفي به وصوره في موضع يذهب من غير تسليم المبيع فإن البيع لازم على الصحيح حتى لو امتنع أحدهما بعده أجبره القاضي. وهذا فيما ثمنه غير معلوم، أما الخبز واللحم فلا يحتاج فيه إلى بيان الثمن كما في البزازية. ومن بيع التعاطي حكماً ما إذا جاء المودع بأمة غير المودعة وقال هذه أمتك والمالك يعلم أنها ليست إياها وحلف فأخذها حل الوطء للمودع وكان بيعاً بالتعاطي وعن أبي يوسف : لو قال للخياط ليست هذه بطانتي فحلف الخياط أنها هي وسعه أخذها وينبغي تقييده فيما إذا كانت العين ملكاً للدافع، أما إذا لم تكن ملكاً له فلا. ومنه قول الدلال للبزاز إن هذا الثوب بدرهم فقال ضعه وكذا بكم تبيع قفيز حنطة إذا كان حاضراً فإنما صار خاطباً لها بالكلام وما وجد من الكلام في المجلس الأول لا يبقى إلى المجلس الثاني فإنما سمع الشهود في المجلس الثاني أحد شطري العقد وسماع الشاهد شطري العقد قبول الخلاصة فقال بدرهم فقال اعزله فعزله فهو بيع وكذا لو قال للقصاب مثله، ومنه لو ردها بخيار عيب والبائع متيقن أنها ليست له فأخذها ورضي فهو بيع بالتعاطي كما في فتح القدير. وعلى هذا لا بد من الرضا في جارية الوديعة وبطانة الخياط، وعلى هذا فالأمر بالعزل أو أنه يكون بالفعل، فالوزن والعزل فعل هو فلا ينبغي إدخاله هنا كما فعل ابن الهمام. وقدمنا في الإيجاب والقبول أنهما بعد عقد فاسد لا ينعقد بهما البيع قبل متاركة الفاسد ففي بيع التعاطي بالأولى، وهو صريح والبزازية أن التعاطي بعد عقد فاسد أو باطل لا ينعقد به البيع لأنه بناء على السابق، وهو محمول على ما ذكرناه وأطلقه فشمل الخسيس والنفيس لأن المعنى يشمل الكل وهو الصحيح المعتمد كما في الهداية وغيرها. وفي والخسيس بما دونه. وفي البزازية : اشترى وقراً بثمانية ثم قال ائت بوقر آخر وألقه هنا ففعل له طلب الثمن . قال لقصاب كم من هذا اللحم بدرهم فقال منوان فأعطى الدرهم وأخذه فهو بيع جائز ولا يعيد الوزن، وإن يحتمل أن يبين البائع قدر الرقم بعشرة دراهم أو أكثر أو أقل. وعن هذا قال الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني : وإن علم بالرقم في المجلس لا ينقلب ذلك العقد جائزاً ولكن إن كان البائع دائماً على الرضا فرضي به This: He said, 'I sold you this for nothing' is void and is not a metaphor for gift even though he expressed its meaning. Similarly, if he said, 'I rented my house to you for a month for nothing' it is not a loan even though he expressed its meaning. Similarly, if he said, 'I bought from you the service of your slave for a month for such and such' it is a void lease. Similarly, if he said, 'I sold you the benefits of the house for a month for such and such' it is a void lease, so the meaning was not considered—the issues are in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya—contrary to when he said, 'I lend you my house for a month for such and such' it is a lease, and similarly, 'I gifted its benefits to you for a month for such and such' considering the meaning. The reality of mu'atah is placing the price and taking the priced item with mutual consent without speech. It indicates from the verbal noun mu'atah that giving from both sides is necessary because it is from the root w-h-y, so it requires occurrence from both sides, like mudarabah, muqasamah, and mukhasamah. This is the view of most scholars as mentioned by al-Tarsusi and al-Halwani gave fatwa accordingly. In al-Bazzaziyya it is the chosen view. It is established in Fath al-Qadir that giving from one side is sufficient. Muhammad stated that the sale of mu'atah is established by taking delivery of one of the two countervalues, and this covers the sold item and the price. His text in al-Jami' states that delivery of the sold item suffices, not negating the other. Al-Kirmani sufficed with delivery of the sold item with specification of the price. As for if he paid the price and did not take the sold item, it is not permissible because the sold item is the principal, except in case of barter. Thus it is established that there are three opinions on the issue. In al-Qamus: al-ta'ati is taking. Similarly in al-Sihah and al-Misbah. It only implies giving from one side and taking from the other, not giving from both sides as al-Tarsusi understood. The origin of the difference arose from the words of Imam Muhammad, for he mentioned bai' al-mu'atah in places: he portrayed it in one place as giving from both sides, so some understood it as a condition; he portrayed it in another place as giving from one side, so some understood that it suffices; he portrayed it in a place as delivery of the sold item, so some understood that delivery of the price does not suffice, as mentioned in al-Dhakhira. One of its forms: two people agree on the price, then the buyer takes the goods and goes away with the seller's consent without paying the price, or the buyer pays the price to the seller then goes away without delivery of the sold item—then the sale is binding correctly, so that if one of them later refuses, the judge compels him. This is when the price is unknown; as for bread and meat, there is no need to specify the price, as in al-Bazzaziyya. From the legal mu'atah: when the depositor comes with a slave-girl other than the deposited one and says 'This is your slave-girl' and the owner knows she is not hers and swears, and he takes her, then intercourse is permissible for the depositor, and it is a sale by mu'atah. From Abu Yusuf: if he said to the tailor, 'This is not my lining' and the tailor swore that it is, it is permissible for him to take it. It should be restricted to when the item is owned by the giver; if not owned by him, then no. From this is the saying of the broker to the cloth-seller: 'This cloth is for a dirham' and he said 'put it down'; similarly, 'For how much do you sell a qafiz of wheat?' if he is present—then he becomes an addressee by speech, and what was of speech in the first session does not remain to the second session; hence the witnesses in the second session hear only one of the two parts of the contract, and hearing the witness of one part of the contract is acceptance. Al-Khulasa: He said, 'For a dirham' and he said, 'Set it aside' and he set it aside, it is a sale. Similarly, if he said to the butcher similarly. From this: if he returned it due to option of defect and the seller is certain it is not his and he took it and was pleased, it is a sale by mu'atah as in Fath al-Qadir. Based on this, consent is necessary in the deposited slave-girl and the tailor's lining. Based on this, the command to set aside or weigh suffices for delivery, so this is a mu'atah sale and there is no delivery from either side because the command to set aside or weigh stands in place of delivery. The offer must be established because it necessitates the precedent of 'I bought' just as 'take' necessitates the precedent of sale, and the weighing of the addressee is acceptance because we said it occurs by action. So weighing and setting aside are actions; therefore it should not be included here as Ibn al-Humam did. We have stated in offer and acceptance that they, after a void contract, do not effect the sale before separation from the void contract, so in bai' al-mu'atah a fortiori. This is explicit in al-Bazzaziyya that mu'atah after a void or invalid contract does not effect the sale because it is based on the previous. This is interpreted as we mentioned, and it is absolute, including the low-value and high-value, because the meaning includes all, and this is the correct and relied-upon view as in al-Hidayah and others. In al-Hawi al-Qudsi: The well-known view is that it is not permissible for valuable items. I say: What he claimed as well-known contradicts the well-known. Al-Nafis is what is high in value like a slave, and al-khasis is what is low in value like bread. Some define al-nafis as the nisab of theft or more, and al-khasis as less than that. In al-Bazzaziyya: He bought a load for eight, then said, 'Bring another load and dump it here' and he did, then he demands the price. He said to the butcher, 'How much of this meat for a dirham?' He said, 'Two mans' and he gave the dirham and took it, it is a valid sale and he does not repeat the weighing. If he weighed and found it short, he reclaims the proportion from the dirham, not from the meat, because the contract was formed on the amount of the sold item. In one session is a condition for the validity of marriage. End. His statement: 'So in bai' al-mu'atah a fortiori.' I say: Mentioned in al-Nihaya, al-Kifaya, and Fath al-Qadir at the statement of al-Hidayah: 'And whoever sells a pile of food, each qafiz for a dirham, the sale is valid in one qafiz according to Abu Hanifa unless he specifies all its qafizes. And the two said it is valid in both ways.' End. For them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], the ignorance is in their hands to remove, and such ignorance does not prevent. But if it is said: Such ignorance does prevent as in sale by number, for it is void even though removing ignorance is in their hands, we say: The sale by number is void because it involves an increase of ignorance entrenched in the core of the contract—ignorance of the price due to a number unknown to the buyer, so it becomes like gambling because it is possible that the seller reveals the amount of the number as ten dirhams or more or less. Regarding this, Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani said: Even if he learns the number in the session, that contract does not become valid, but if the seller is continuously content and the buyer is pleased, a new contract is formed initially by mutual consent. In al-Fath he expressed it as mu'atah and sometimes as mutual consent, and mu'atah means the same. Then the restriction of the issue—namely the non-conclusion of sale by mu'atah after a void contract before separation—applies if that is after the session. As for if they mutually consent in it, it is concluded without separation from the first void contract, as is explicit in the statement of Shams al-A'imma, unless it is restricted to after separation from the first, so let it be considered. See what comes in the commentary on his statement 'and if he sold a flock or a garment' and perhaps there are two opinions on the issue. Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, commenting on the definition and conditions of bai' al-mu'atah (transaction by mutual giving). The author first clarifies that mere expression of meaning does not change the legal nature of a contract; for example, saying 'I sold you this for nothing' is void and not a gift, and saying 'I rented you my house for a month for nothing' is not a loan. Then he defines the reality of mu'atah: placing the price and taking the priced item with mutual consent without verbal offer and acceptance. The key dispute is whether mutual giving from both sides is required. Most scholars, including al-Tarsusi and al-Halwani, hold that it is necessary, based on the verbal noun pattern mu'afalah which implies reciprocal action. Others, like the author of Fath al-Qadir, consider giving from one side sufficient. This difference originates from Imam Muhammad's various statements: in one place he described mu'atah as giving from both sides, in another as one side, and in another as delivery of the sold item. The author discusses three opinions and then provides examples: taking goods with consent without payment, or paying without taking goods, constitute valid mu'atah. Further examples include depository scenarios and tailor's lining. The passage then addresses that mu'atah after a void contract is not valid unless separated from the first contract, and that mu'atah is valid for both low-value and high-value items, contrary to the claim in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Finally, a tangent discussion on bai' al-raqam (sale by unknown number) clarifies that it is void due to excessive ignorance, but if both parties consent in the session, a new contract can be formed by mu'atah.

Key Terms
المفاعلة (al-mufa'alah): A verbal noun pattern indicating reciprocal action between two parties.
التناول (al-tanaawul): Taking; the definition of al-ta'ati in lexicons, implying one-sided action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school, bai' al-mu'atah is completed when:
A) Both parties have verbally declared offer and acceptance.
B) Both the buyer and seller have physically delivered their countervalues.
C) One of the two parties has delivered their countervalue with mutual consent.
D) The buyer has taken the goods and the seller has received the price in the same session.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has three views on whether both parties must give in mu'atah; the mufta bihi is that one side suffices, but the dispute is legitimate and affects practical rulings on ownership transfer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Sale by Mutual Transaction: Ruling and Conditions
Explaining the validity and conditions of sale by mutual transaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bay' al-ta'ati, identify its conditions, and explain why it is valid for both low-value and precious items.

Core Idea
A sale by mutual action is valid when mutual consent is established, regardless of the item's value.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on bay' al-ta'ati — a sale concluded by mutual action without formal offer and acceptance. The Hanafi school affirms its validity based on the principle that mutual consent is the essence of a contract. We will explore the conditions, the dispute concerning valuable items, and how the relied-upon view encompasses all merchandise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Bay' al-ta'ati is a sale concluded by mutual action (e.g., handing over goods and taking price) without verbal offer and acceptance. The Hanafi ruling: it is a valid (sahih) sale.
2
Locate the condition
The key condition is mutual consent (rida). Actions that clearly indicate consent, like handing over and taking, are sufficient.
Formal words are not required.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is that the essence of a sale is mutual consent, and actions can convey consent just as words do. The Quran (4:29) 'trade by mutual consent' supports this, though not explicitly quoted here.
The passage argues by talil: the meaning (consent) applies to all cases.
4
Note the dispute
Some scholars (as in al-Hawi al-Qudsi) restrict ta'ati to low-value items (khasis) and disallow it for precious items (nafis). The Hanafi response: the correct view is that it is valid for all items, and the claimed restriction is contrary to the well-known position.
Precious items are defined as those reaching the nisab of theft or high value like a slave.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, any transaction where goods and price are exchanged with mutual consent (e.g., buying bread by leaving money and taking bread) is a valid sale by mutual transaction.
Also, after a void contract, ta'ati is not valid until separation from the void contract.

فقال بدرهم فقال اعزله فعزله فهو بيع وكذا لو قال للقصاب مثله، ومنه لو ردها بخيار عيب والبائع متيقن أنها ليست له فأخذها ورضي فهو بيع بالتعاطي كما في فتح القدير. وعلى هذا لا بد من الرضا في جارية الوديعة وبطانة الخياط، وعلى هذا فالأمر بالعزل أو الوزن يكفي عن القبض فهذا بيع معاطاة ولا قبض فيه من أحد الجانبين لكون الأمر بالعزل والوزن قائماً مقام القبض ويجب أن يقام الإيجاب لاقتضائه سابقة اشتريت كاقتضاء خذ سابقة البيع ووزن المخاطب قبول لما قدمنا أنه يكون بالفعل، فالوزن والعزل فعل هو فلا ينبغي إدخاله هنا كما فعل ابن الهمام. وقدمنا في الإيجاب والقبول أنهما بعد عقد فاسد لا ينعقد بهما البيع قبل متاركة الفاسد ففي بيع التعاطي بالأولى، وهو صريح والبزازية أن التعاطي بعد عقد فاسد أو باطل لا ينعقد به البيع لأنه بناء على السابق، وهو محمول على ما ذكرناه وأطلقه فشمل الخسيس والنفيس لأن المعنى يشمل الكل وهو الصحيح المعتمد كما في الهداية وغيرها. وفي الحاوي القدسي : المشهور أنه لا يجوز في نفائس الأشياء اهـ . قلت : وما ادعاه من المشهور فخلاف المشهور والنفيس ما كثر ثمنه كالعبد، والخسيس ما قل ثمنه كالخبز. ومنهم من حد النفيس بنصاب السرقة فأكثر، والخسيس بما دونه. وفي البزازية : اشترى وقراً بثمانية ثم قال ائت بوقر آخر وألقه هنا ففعل له طلب الثمن . قال لقصاب كم من هذا اللحم بدرهم فقال منوان فأعطى الدرهم وأخذه فهو بيع جائز ولا يعيد الوزن، وإن وزنه فوجده أنقص رجع بقدره من الدرهم لا من اللحم لأن الانعقاد بقدر المبيع في مجلس واحد شرط الجواز النكاح .اهـ.
He said: 'For a dirham,' and he said: 'Set it aside.' So he set it aside, and it is a sale. Likewise, if he said to the butcher similarly. And among them, if he returned it due to a defect option, and the seller is certain it is not his, so he took it and was content, it is a sale by mutual transaction, as in Fath al-Qadir. On this, consent is necessary in the case of the deposit slave-girl and the lining of the tailor. On this, the order to set aside or weigh suffices for possession; this is a sale by mutual transaction, and there is no possession from either side because the order to set aside and weigh stands in place of possession. And the offer must be established because it requires a prior 'I bought', just as 'take' requires a prior sale, and the weighing by the addressee is acceptance because we have stated that it occurs by act. So weighing and setting aside are acts, so it should not be entered here as Ibn al-Humam did. And we have stated regarding offer and acceptance that they occur after a void contract; the sale is not concluded by them before separating from the void [contract]. So in a sale by mutual transaction, it is more so. And it is explicit in al-Bazzaziyyah that mutual transaction after a void or invalid contract does not conclude a sale because it is based on the prior [contract]. And it is carried on what we mentioned, and it is absolute, so it includes both low-value and precious [items] because the meaning includes all, and it is the correct and relied-upon [view] as in al-Hidayah and others. In al-Hawi al-Qudsi: 'The well-known [view] is that it is not permissible for precious things.' End. I say: What he claims as well-known contradicts the well-known. Precious is that whose price is high, like a slave; low-value is that whose price is low, like bread. Some define precious as the nisab of theft or more, low-value as less than that. In al-Bazzaziyyah: He bought a load for eight, then said: 'Bring another load and throw it here,' so he did; he demands the price. He said to a butcher: 'How much of this meat for a dirham?' He said: 'Two manns.' So he gave the dirham and took it; it is a valid sale and he does not repeat the weighing. If he weighed it and found it less, he recovers the amount from the dirham, not from the meat, because the contract is concluded for the amount of the sold item in one session; it is a condition for validity. 'al-Nikah' end.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling of sale by mutual transaction (bay' al-ta'ati). The author begins by giving examples: if a buyer says 'set it aside' and the seller does so, it constitutes a sale. Similarly, if a buyer returns an item due to a defect and the seller accepts it knowing it is not his, it is a valid sale by mutual transaction. The key condition is mutual consent (rida). The author then states that an order to set aside or weigh suffices for possession, and the weighing itself can be considered acceptance. The passage also addresses the validity of mutual transaction after a void or invalid contract: it is not valid until the parties separate from the void contract, because it would be based on the prior invalid transaction. However, this is not a general rule; it applies only before separation. The author then discusses whether mutual transaction is valid for valuable items. The relied-upon view is that it is valid for both low-value and precious items, contrary to a claim that it is not permissible for precious items. The definition of precious is given as items of high value (e.g., a slave) or those reaching the nisab of theft. Finally, an example from al-Bazzaziyyah is given: a buyer gives a dirham for two manns of meat; the sale is valid without re-weighing. If the weight is deficient, the buyer recovers proportionally from the price, not from the meat, because the contract is concluded for the specific amount in the session. The mention of 'nikah' at the end appears to be an error in the text.

Key Terms
بيع التعاطي (bay' al-ta'ati): A sale concluded by mutual action (giving and taking) without formal verbal offer and acceptance.
الخسيس (al-khasis): Low-value items, such as bread.
النفيس (al-nafis): High-value items, such as a slave or items reaching the nisab of theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, is sale by mutual transaction (bay' al-ta'ati) valid for precious items such as a slave?
A) Yes, it is valid for all items, regardless of value.
B) No, it is only valid for low-value items.
C) It is valid only if the price is paid immediately.
D) It is invalid altogether.

Key Takeaway
Sale by mutual action (bay' al-ta'ati) is valid for all items when mutual consent is established, and it is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Standing Up Before Acceptance Voids the Offer
Ruling with ta‘līl on standing before acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that standing up before acceptance voids the offer and explain the legal reasoning (ta‘līl) based on the composite cause.

Core Idea
Standing up before acceptance voids the offer because the offer is only one part of a composite cause.

In a sale negotiation, the moment an offer is made, it is not yet binding. The Hanafi school holds that if the offeror stands up before the other party accepts, the offer is automatically void. This ruling is not arbitrary; it is based on a precise understanding of the structure of legal causes. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning behind this rule and see how it applies in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the offeror standing up from the meeting place (al-qiyām ‘an al-majlis) before the offeree accepts. The ruling is that the offer becomes void (bāṭil).
2
Understand the legal cause (ʿilla) of the contract
The contract's validity depends on a cause composed of two parts: the offer (ījāb) and the acceptance (qabūl). Each part is necessary; the cause is not complete until both occur.
The offer alone has the status of a sabab (means), not the complete ʿilla.
3
Apply the principle of the composite cause
When a ruling depends on a cause with two attributes, the first attribute has the ruling of a cause only when the second is present. Before acceptance, the offer does not yet have the full effect of a cause. Standing up before acceptance is therefore a refusal to complete the cause, which voids the offer.
The author states: 'standing is a refusal to complete the cause, not an annulment of it.'
4
Distinguish from Shafi‘i position
Some schools (e.g., Shafi‘i) hold that the offer remains valid until the parties physically separate or one explicitly revokes; standing alone is not withdrawal. The Hanafi view considers standing as a clear indicator of withdrawal.
This difference stems from different interpretations of the Prophetic hadith on ‘choice of the session’ (khiyār al-majlis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأي قام عن المجلس قبل القبول بطل الايجاب لكونه امتناعاً عن إتمام العلة لا إبطالاً لها، وهذا لأن إيجاب البائع أحد شطري العلة والحكم إذا تعلق بعلة ذات وصفين كان للأول حكم السبب وللثاني حكم العلة، فلما لم يكن للأول قبل القبول حكم العلة لا يكون إبطال الإيجاب بالقيام الانعقاد قبل متاركة الأول وعبارة الخلاصة : اشترى رجل من وسائدي وسائد ووجوه الطنافس وهي غير منسوجة بعد ولم يضربا له أجلاً لم يجز . فلو نسج الوسائد ووجوه الطنافس وسلم إلى المشتري لا هذا بيعاً بالتعاطي لأنهما يعلمان بحكم ذلك البيع السابق وأنه وقع باطلاً.
His saying: (And if he stands up from the session before acceptance, the offer is void, because it is a refusal to complete the cause, not an annulment of it. And this is because the seller's offer is one of the two parts of the cause, and if a ruling is attached to a cause with two attributes, the first has the ruling of a cause, and the second has the ruling of a cause. So when the first does not have the ruling of a cause before acceptance, the offer is not annulled by standing; rather, it prevents the conclusion of the contract before separation from the first. And the expression of al-Khulasah: A man bought cushions and tapestry faces from a pillowseller, and they were not yet woven, and they did not set a term; it is not valid. If he wove the cushions and tapestry faces and delivered them to the buyer, this is not a sale by mutual transaction, because they know the ruling of that prior sale and that it occurred invalidly.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage explains a key principle in offer and acceptance: if the person making the offer stands up from the meeting place before the other party accepts, the offer becomes void. The reason is that standing up is a refusal to complete the contract, not an annulment of the offer itself. The author uses a technical analogy: the offer is one of the two parts of the cause ('illa) for the contract; the ruling (the contract's validity) depends on both parts. Before acceptance, the offer has the status of a cause (sabab), but not the full cause. Therefore, standing up does not annul the offer retroactively; it merely prevents the contract from being concluded because the offeror has withdrawn. The author then cites al-Khulasah with an example: if a person buys cushions that are not yet woven and no term is set, the sale is invalid. If the seller later weaves them and delivers them, it is not a sale by mutual transaction because both parties know the prior sale was invalid.

Key Terms
القيام عن المجلس (al-qiyam 'an al-majlis): Physically standing up from the meeting place where the contract is being negotiated.
العلة (al-'illa): The legal cause; here, the cause of the contract is composed of both offer and acceptance.
السبب (al-sabab): A means or cause that is not fully effective until its complement is present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale negotiation, if the seller makes an offer and then stands up before the buyer accepts, what is the Hanafi ruling on the offer?
A) The offer remains valid until the buyer accepts.
B) The offer becomes void and cannot be accepted later.
C) The offer is suspended until they sit again.
D) The offer is void only if the seller leaves the room.

Key Takeaway
Standing up before acceptance voids the offer in Hanafi fiqh because the offer is only one part of a composite cause; standing constitutes withdrawal.

Bay' al-Ta'ati: Validity and Core Conditions
Explains the ruling and condition of sale by exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bay' al-ta'ati and identify the conditions required for its validity.

Core Idea
A sale is valid by mutual delivery and receipt without words if consent and knowledge are present.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, a sale does not always require a formal verbal offer and acceptance. The school recognizes sale by exchange (bay' al-ta'ati), where the contract is concluded by mutual delivery of the goods and price. This lesson examines the basic ruling from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, focusing on the example of a meat purchase and the essential conditions of consent, knowledge, and possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The text discusses sale by exchange (bay' al-ta'ati) where no verbal ijab/qabul occurs. The ruling is that such a sale is valid (sahih) when conditions are met.
2
Analyze the example
The example of meat: buyer says 'I take it, weigh' and seller weighs. The sale is not final until possession (qabd) occurs with permission. Why? Because mutual consent is not yet certain.
Possession with permission completes the contract.
3
Extract the conditions
The text implies conditions: mutual consent (rida), knowledge of price quantity/description, knowledge of subject matter, and possession (qabd) with permission. Without these, the sale is not valid.
Consent is paramount; even possession without consent does not complete the sale.
4
Contrast with other schools
The Hanafi position differs from the Shafi'i school, which requires verbal expression. Understand why the Hanafis accept action as sufficient evidence of consent.
The difference stems from usul: Hanafis prioritize customary practice (urf) and apparent consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبيع المجلس قبل القبول بطل الإيجاب ولا بد من معرفة قدر ووصف ثمن غير مشار لا المعطى. قال كيف تبيع اللحم قال ثلاثة أرطال بدرهم فقال أخذت فزن فله أن يزن ولا يلزم، وإن وزن فله أن لا يعطي وللمشتري أن لا يأخذ وإن قبضه المشتري أو جعله البائع في وعاء بإذن المشتري تم البيع وفيه انعقاده بالإعطاء من جانب حلف لا يشتري أو لا فباع أو اشترى بالتعاطي قيل وقيل .اهـ. وقدمنا أنه لو أمره بالوزن ولم يبين موضعاً فوزن له لا يكون بيعاً ولو بين له كان بيعاً، وقد ذكره في فتح القدير هنا على العكس فليتأمل. واعلم أن الإقالة تنعقد بالتعاطي أيضا من أحد الجانبين على الصحيح كالبيع كما في البزازية. وفي القنية : دفع إلى بائع حنطة خمسة دنانير ليأخذ منه حنطة وقال له بكم تبيعها فقال مائة بدينار فسكت المشتري ثم طلب منه الحنطة ليأخذها فقال البائع غداً أدفع إليك ولم يجر بينهما بيع وذهب المشتري فجاء غداً ليأخذ الحنطة وقد تغير السعر فليس للبائع أن يمنعها منه بل عليه أن يدفعها بالسعر الأول. قال رضي الله عنه : وفي هذه الواقعة أربعة مسائل : أحدها الانعقاد بالتعاطي الثانية الانعقاد به في الخسيس والنفيس وهو الصحيح الثالثة الانعقاد به من جانب واحد. والرابعة كما ينعقد بإعطاء المبيع ينعقد بإعطاء الثمن اهـ. قلت: وفيها مسألة خامسة أنه ينعقد به ولو تأخرت معرفة المثمن لكون دفع الثمن قبل معرفته. وفي المجتبى معزياً إلى النصاب : عليه دين فطالب رب الدين به فبعث إليه شعيراً قدراً معلوما وقال خذه بسعر البلد والسعر لهما وقال معلوم كان بيعاً وإن لم يعلماه فلا . ومن بيع التعاطي تسليم المشتري ما اشترى إلى من يطلبه بالشفعة في موضع لا شفعة فيه، وكذا تسليم الوكيل بعدما صار شراؤه لنفسه إلى الموكل إذا قبضه الآمر وأنكر الأمر وقد اشترى له؛ كذا في المجتبى وذكر مسألتي الوديعة والخياط المتقدمتين. ومنه لو ادعى بيعاً وبرهن بشهود زور والقضاء إذا به على قول أبي يوسف ؛ كذا في المجتبى. يعني وإن قالا بأن القضاء بشهادة الزور لا ينفذ باطنا يقولا بالانعقاد بالتعاطي بعده. ثم اعلم أنه إنما ينعقد بالتعاطي بشرط أن بعدم الرضا، فلو قبض الدراهم الثمن وأخذ صاحبها البطاطيخ والبائع يقول لا أعطيكها أو حلف فإنه لا يصح البيع وتمامه في القنية والله أعلم
The sale: before acceptance the offer is invalidated, and it is necessary to know the quantity and description of the price, not the [price] given. He said: How do you sell the meat? He replied: three pounds for a dirham. So he said: I take it, weigh it. He has the right to weigh and is not obliged; and if he weighs, he may refuse to give it, and the buyer may refuse to take it. But if the buyer takes possession, or the seller puts it in a container with the buyer's permission, the sale is complete. And included in it: its conclusion by giving from one side in case of an oath not to buy or sell — there are two opinions. And we previously mentioned that if he ordered him to weigh without specifying a place, and he weighed for him, it is not a sale; but if he specified a place, it is a sale. And it was mentioned in Fath al-Qadir here oppositely, so let it be considered. And know that rescission (iqalah) is also concluded by exchange (ta'ati) from one side according to the correct view, like a sale, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And in al-Qunyah: A person gave a wheat seller five dinars to take wheat from him, and said to him: For how much do you sell it? He replied: a hundred for a dinar. The buyer remained silent, then asked for the wheat to take it. The seller said: Tomorrow I will deliver to you, and no sale had taken place between them. The buyer left, and came the next day to take the wheat, and the price had changed. The seller has no right to withhold it from him; rather he must deliver it at the original price. Imam Radhi said: In this incident there are four issues: First, conclusion by exchange (ta'ati); second, conclusion by it for both cheap and valuable goods — and that is correct; third, conclusion by it from one side; fourth, just as it is concluded by delivery of the sold item, it is concluded by delivery of the price. I said: There is a fifth issue that it is concluded by it even if knowledge of the price [or object] is delayed, because the price was paid before knowing it. And in al-Mujtaba, citing al-Nisab: He owed a debt, and the creditor demanded it, so he sent him a known quantity of barley and said: Take it at the market price, and the price was known to both? He said: It is a sale if known, otherwise not. And from the sales by exchange (ta'ati) is the buyer delivering what he bought to one who claims preemption in a place where there is no preemption; likewise the delivery of the agent after his purchase became for himself to the principal, if the orderer received it and denied the order while he had purchased for him; so in al-Mujtaba, and the two previous issues of deposit and tailor were mentioned. And from it, if someone claims a sale and proves it with false witnesses, and the judge rules according to it, on Abu Yusuf's view; so in al-Mujtaba. Meaning, although they say that judgment based on false testimony is not effective inwardly, they say that the contract is concluded by exchange (ta'ati) afterwards. Then know that it is only concluded by exchange (ta'ati) on the condition of [mutual] consent. So if he takes the dirhams as price and the owner takes the watermelons while the seller says: I will not give them to you, or swears, then the sale is not valid. The rest is in al-Qunyah. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the rules of bay' al-ta'ati (sale by exchange) — a contract concluded by mutual delivery and taking of the goods and price without a formal verbal offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). The author begins by quoting the matn, which states that if a person stands up from the session before acceptance, the offer is invalidated, and that it is necessary to know the quantity and description of the price. The commentary then focuses on the latter part, illustrating with a hypothetical: a buyer asks the seller three pounds of meat for a dirham; the seller says 'weigh it' and the buyer says 'I take it, weigh.' Here, the buyer is allowed to weigh and is not bound to buy; after weighing, the seller may refuse to hand it over, and the buyer may refuse to take it. However, if the buyer takes possession (qabd) or the seller puts the meat into a container with the buyer's permission, the sale is complete. This shows that delivery and receipt with mutual consent constitute a valid sale. The author then discusses the binding nature of the contract: if a person swore not to buy or sell and then concluded a transaction by ta'ati, there are two opinions. Earlier it was noted that if the seller orders weighing without specifying a place, it is not a sale; if a place is specified, it is a sale — though Fath al-Qadir reverses this, so caution is needed. The ruling on iqalah (rescission) is similar: it may be concluded by ta'ati from one side. The author then cites a case from al-Qunyah: a person pays five dinars for wheat at a specified price without formal offer/acceptance; the seller promises delivery the next day. If the price changes overnight, the seller must deliver at the original price. From this, four rulings are derived: (1) contract by ta'ati is valid; (2) it applies to both cheap and expensive goods; (3) it can be concluded from one side (i.e., delivery of the sold item alone or price alone); (4) it can be concluded by payment of the price just as by delivery of the sold item. The author adds a fifth: it is valid even if knowledge of the price or object is delayed because the price was paid first. The passage then gives more examples from al-Mujtaba: a debtor sends barley to his creditor; if the price is known to both, it is a sale; if not, it is not. Other examples include delivery to a preemptor in a place with no preemption, and an agent's delivery to his principal. The final condition is that ta'ati requires mutual consent; if the seller refuses or swears not to give the goods, the sale does not occur. The author cautions that in the case of false testimony, the judgment is valid externally per Abu Yusuf, but inwardly the contract may still be considered concluded by ta'ati if the parties act accordingly.

Key Terms
بيع التعاطي (bay' al-ta'ati): A sale concluded by mutual delivery and acceptance of the goods and price without verbal offer and acceptance.
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement, which can also be concluded by ta'ati.
رضا (rida): Mutual consent of both contracting parties, a necessary condition for the validity of ta'ati.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale by exchange (ta'ati), which of the following is NOT required for the sale to be valid?
A) Mutual consent of the parties
B) Knowledge of the price quantity and description
C) Verbal offer and acceptance
D) Possession of the goods with permission

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-ta'ati is a valid method of sale in the Hanafi school, requiring only mutual consent, knowledge of price and subject, and possession with permission.

Bay' al-Ta'ati: Expanded Cases and Conditions
Lists applications and rulings derived from bay' al-ta'ati.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of bay' al-ta'ati to specific scenarios including iqalah, delayed knowledge, and disputes.

Core Idea
Bay' al-ta'ati applies to rescission, valuable goods, and even one-sided delivery, as long as consent is clear.

Having established the basic ruling, the passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq now explores several practical applications and nuances of bay' al-ta'ati. These include its use in rescission (iqalah), its applicability to both cheap and valuable goods, the possibility of concluding the contract from one side, and the effect of delayed knowledge. This lesson will unpack these expansions, helping you apply the rules to real-world situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the additional rulings
The passage presents rulings beyond the basic: (1) iqalah (rescission) can be concluded by ta'ati from one side; (2) ta'ati works for both cheap and valuable goods; (3) it can be concluded by delivery of the price alone; (4) it is valid even if knowledge of the object is delayed because the price was paid first.
These are derived from the al-Qunyah case.
2
Analyze the al-Qunyah case
A buyer pays five dinars for wheat without a formal contract, and the seller promises delivery next day. The price changes, but the seller must deliver at the original price. This shows that the exchange created a binding obligation.
The contract is concluded by payment and agreement on price.
3
Consider the condition of consent critically
The passage ends with a strong emphasis: ta'ati only works with mutual consent. If the seller refuses or swears not to give, the sale is invalid. This means that the outward act of taking may not suffice if there is clear indication of dissent.
Consent must be established; mere possession is not enough if contested.
4
Apply to the other examples
The passage mentions delivery to a preemptor in a no-preemption zone, agent delivery, and false witness cases. Each illustrates that ta'ati can serve to transfer ownership when consent is inferred from the circumstances.
These are specific applications from al-Mujtaba.

يبيع المجلس قبل القبول بطل الإيجاب ولا بد من معرفة قدر ووصف ثمن غير مشار لا المعطى. قال كيف تبيع اللحم قال ثلاثة أرطال بدرهم فقال أخذت فزن فله أن يزن ولا يلزم، وإن وزن فله أن لا يعطي وللمشتري أن لا يأخذ وإن قبضه المشتري أو جعله البائع في وعاء بإذن المشتري تم البيع وفيه انعقاده بالإعطاء من جانب حلف لا يشتري أو لا فباع أو اشترى بالتعاطي قيل وقيل .اهـ. وقدمنا أنه لو أمره بالوزن ولم يبين موضعاً فوزن له لا يكون بيعاً ولو بين له كان بيعاً، وقد ذكره في فتح القدير هنا على العكس فليتأمل. واعلم أن الإقالة تنعقد بالتعاطي أيضا من أحد الجانبين على الصحيح كالبيع كما في البزازية. وفي القنية : دفع إلى بائع حنطة خمسة دنانير ليأخذ منه حنطة وقال له بكم تبيعها فقال مائة بدينار فسكت المشتري ثم طلب منه الحنطة ليأخذها فقال البائع غداً أدفع إليك ولم يجر بينهما بيع وذهب المشتري فجاء غداً ليأخذ الحنطة وقد تغير السعر فليس للبائع أن يمنعها منه بل عليه أن يدفعها بالسعر الأول. قال رضي الله عنه : وفي هذه الواقعة أربعة مسائل : أحدها الانعقاد بالتعاطي الثانية الانعقاد به في الخسيس والنفيس وهو الصحيح الثالثة الانعقاد به من جانب واحد. والرابعة كما ينعقد بإعطاء المبيع ينعقد بإعطاء الثمن اهـ. قلت: وفيها مسألة خامسة أنه ينعقد به ولو تأخرت معرفة المثمن لكون دفع الثمن قبل معرفته. وفي المجتبى معزياً إلى النصاب : عليه دين فطالب رب الدين به فبعث إليه شعيراً قدراً معلوما وقال خذه بسعر البلد والسعر لهما وقال معلوم كان بيعاً وإن لم يعلماه فلا . ومن بيع التعاطي تسليم المشتري ما اشترى إلى من يطلبه بالشفعة في موضع لا شفعة فيه، وكذا تسليم الوكيل بعدما صار شراؤه لنفسه إلى الموكل إذا قبضه الآمر وأنكر الأمر وقد اشترى له؛ كذا في المجتبى وذكر مسألتي الوديعة والخياط المتقدمتين. ومنه لو ادعى بيعاً وبرهن بشهود زور والقضاء إذا به على قول أبي يوسف ؛ كذا في المجتبى. يعني وإن قالا بأن القضاء بشهادة الزور لا ينفذ باطنا يقولا بالانعقاد بالتعاطي بعده. ثم اعلم أنه إنما ينعقد بالتعاطي بشرط أن بعدم الرضا، فلو قبض الدراهم الثمن وأخذ صاحبها البطاطيخ والبائع يقول لا أعطيكها أو حلف فإنه لا يصح البيع وتمامه في القنية والله أعلم
The sale: before acceptance the offer is invalidated, and it is necessary to know the quantity and description of the price, not the [price] given. He said: How do you sell the meat? He replied: three pounds for a dirham. So he said: I take it, weigh it. He has the right to weigh and is not obliged; and if he weighs, he may refuse to give it, and the buyer may refuse to take it. But if the buyer takes possession, or the seller puts it in a container with the buyer's permission, the sale is complete. And included in it: its conclusion by giving from one side in case of an oath not to buy or sell — there are two opinions. And we previously mentioned that if he ordered him to weigh without specifying a place, and he weighed for him, it is not a sale; but if he specified a place, it is a sale. And it was mentioned in Fath al-Qadir here oppositely, so let it be considered. And know that rescission (iqalah) is also concluded by exchange (ta'ati) from one side according to the correct view, like a sale, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And in al-Qunyah: A person gave a wheat seller five dinars to take wheat from him, and said to him: For how much do you sell it? He replied: a hundred for a dinar. The buyer remained silent, then asked for the wheat to take it. The seller said: Tomorrow I will deliver to you, and no sale had taken place between them. The buyer left, and came the next day to take the wheat, and the price had changed. The seller has no right to withhold it from him; rather he must deliver it at the original price. Imam Radhi said: In this incident there are four issues: First, conclusion by exchange (ta'ati); second, conclusion by it for both cheap and valuable goods — and that is correct; third, conclusion by it from one side; fourth, just as it is concluded by delivery of the sold item, it is concluded by delivery of the price. I said: There is a fifth issue that it is concluded by it even if knowledge of the price [or object] is delayed, because the price was paid before knowing it. And in al-Mujtaba, citing al-Nisab: He owed a debt, and the creditor demanded it, so he sent him a known quantity of barley and said: Take it at the market price, and the price was known to both? He said: It is a sale if known, otherwise not. And from the sales by exchange (ta'ati) is the buyer delivering what he bought to one who claims preemption in a place where there is no preemption; likewise the delivery of the agent after his purchase became for himself to the principal, if the orderer received it and denied the order while he had purchased for him; so in al-Mujtaba, and the two previous issues of deposit and tailor were mentioned. And from it, if someone claims a sale and proves it with false witnesses, and the judge rules according to it, on Abu Yusuf's view; so in al-Mujtaba. Meaning, although they say that judgment based on false testimony is not effective inwardly, they say that the contract is concluded by exchange (ta'ati) afterwards. Then know that it is only concluded by exchange (ta'ati) on the condition of [mutual] consent. So if he takes the dirhams as price and the owner takes the watermelons while the seller says: I will not give them to you, or swears, then the sale is not valid. The rest is in al-Qunyah. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the rules of bay' al-ta'ati (sale by exchange) — a contract concluded by mutual delivery and taking of the goods and price without a formal verbal offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). The author begins by quoting the matn, which states that if a person stands up from the session before acceptance, the offer is invalidated, and that it is necessary to know the quantity and description of the price. The commentary then focuses on the latter part, illustrating with a hypothetical: a buyer asks the seller three pounds of meat for a dirham; the seller says 'weigh it' and the buyer says 'I take it, weigh.' Here, the buyer is allowed to weigh and is not bound to buy; after weighing, the seller may refuse to hand it over, and the buyer may refuse to take it. However, if the buyer takes possession (qabd) or the seller puts the meat into a container with the buyer's permission, the sale is complete. This shows that delivery and receipt with mutual consent constitute a valid sale. The author then discusses the binding nature of the contract: if a person swore not to buy or sell and then concluded a transaction by ta'ati, there are two opinions. Earlier it was noted that if the seller orders weighing without specifying a place, it is not a sale; if a place is specified, it is a sale — though Fath al-Qadir reverses this, so caution is needed. The ruling on iqalah (rescission) is similar: it may be concluded by ta'ati from one side. The author then cites a case from al-Qunyah: a person pays five dinars for wheat at a specified price without formal offer/acceptance; the seller promises delivery the next day. If the price changes overnight, the seller must deliver at the original price. From this, four rulings are derived: (1) contract by ta'ati is valid; (2) it applies to both cheap and expensive goods; (3) it can be concluded from one side (i.e., delivery of the sold item alone or price alone); (4) it can be concluded by payment of the price just as by delivery of the sold item. The author adds a fifth: it is valid even if knowledge of the price or object is delayed because the price was paid first. The passage then gives more examples from al-Mujtaba: a debtor sends barley to his creditor; if the price is known to both, it is a sale; if not, it is not. Other examples include delivery to a preemptor in a place with no preemption, and an agent's delivery to his principal. The final condition is that ta'ati requires mutual consent; if the seller refuses or swears not to give the goods, the sale does not occur. The author cautions that in the case of false testimony, the judgment is valid externally per Abu Yusuf, but inwardly the contract may still be considered concluded by ta'ati if the parties act accordingly.

Key Terms
بيع التعاطي (bay' al-ta'ati): A sale concluded by mutual delivery and acceptance of the goods and price without verbal offer and acceptance.
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement, which can also be concluded by ta'ati.
رضا (rida): Mutual consent of both contracting parties, a necessary condition for the validity of ta'ati.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the al-Qunyah case, what happens if the buyer pays the price but the seller delays delivery and the market price changes?
A) The seller must deliver at the original price.
B) The seller can charge the new market price.
C) The sale is void because of the delay.
D) The buyer can cancel the sale.

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-ta'ati extends to rescission, valuable goods, one-sided delivery, and delayed knowledge, but always requires mutual consent.
