Purchase of a Pearl in Its Shell: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on buying a pearl without extracting it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi ruling on buying a pearl in its shell and explain why the fatwa follows Imam Muhammad over Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A pearl inseparable from its shell causes excessive gharar, making the sale invalid even with an option to inspect.

This lesson examines a nuanced disagreement between two Hanafi giants—Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad—regarding the sale of a concealed pearl. At stake is whether the option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'yah) can cure the uncertainty when the object cannot be examined without destruction. The passage decisively resolves the dispute by declaring Imam Muhammad's view as the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), grounding it in the natural inseparability of the pearl from its shell.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed transaction
Recognise the subject of sale: a pearl (lu'lu'ah) inside its shell (sadafah) without extraction. The uncertainty lies in the inability to examine the pearl without breaking the shell.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position and proof
Abu Yusuf permits the sale with khiyar al-ru'yah (option on sighting). His reasoning: the gharar is minor because the buyer can inspect the pearl after purchase and then accept or reject.
He treats the shell as a container that can be opened later.
3
State Imam Muhammad's position and proof
Imam Muhammad rules the sale invalid (la yajuz). His reasoning: the pearl is part of the shell's creation (minha khilqah); to inspect it, the shell must be destroyed, making the gharar excessive and incurable by khiyar.
The shell and pearl are inseparable in substance, not just packaging.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Yusuf, the sale is valid but subject to cancellation; under Muhammad, the sale is void from the outset and cannot be rectified. This affects the legal status of any payment made.
5
Note the tarjih and its basis
The text states 'wa 'alayhi al-fatwa' (the fatwa is upon it). The preferred view is Imam Muhammad's, and the cause given is that the pearl is created from the shell—i.e., the obscurity is inherent and excessive.
This tarjih follows the principle that khiyar al-ru'yah cannot cure gharar fahish (excessive uncertainty).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو مراد المحقق. وفي الخانية ولو اشترى لؤلؤة في صدفة قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى : يجوز البيع وله الخيار إذا رأى. وقال محمد رحمه الله تعالى : لا يجوز وعليه الفتوى اهـ. وهكذا في الولوالجية معللاً للفتوى بأنها منه خلقة.
And it is the intent of the Muhaqqiq. In al-Khaniyyah: If someone buys a pearl in its shell, Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The sale is valid and he has the option when he sees it. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is not valid, and the fatwa is upon that. And thus in al-Walwajiyyah, giving as cause for the fatwa that it is created from it.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of a pearl still in its shell. The issue is whether the sale is valid given the unknown nature of the pearl. Abu Yusuf holds that the sale is valid but the buyer has the option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'yah) because when he sees the pearl he can either accept or reject it. Imam Muhammad holds that the sale is invalid from the outset because the pearl is completely unknown—it is part of the shell's creation and cannot be examined without destruction. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Imam Muhammad's view, as stated in al-Khaniyyah and al-Walwajiyyah. The reason given is 'annahu minha khilqah, meaning the pearl is integral to the shell and cannot be separated without breaking it, which is akin to selling a hidden object with excessive gharar.

Key Terms
لؤلؤة (lu'lu'ah): A pearl; the object of sale in the dispute.
صدفة (sadafah): A shell; the outer covering of the pearl that conceals it.
خيار (khiyar): The option to confirm or cancel a sale upon seeing the item (khiyar al-ru'yah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on buying a pearl in its shell without extracting it?
A) The sale is valid because the buyer has the option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'yah).
B) The sale is invalid because the pearl is inseparable from the shell (gharar).
C) The sale is valid only if the price is less than market value.
D) The sale is makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa holds that a pearl inseparable from its shell cannot be sold due to excessive gharar, even with an option to inspect, because inspection requires destruction.

Types of Dirhams and Option for Defective Coins
Classification of dirhams and buyer's option for defective currency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify dirhams into the four Hanafi types and apply the rule of khiyar al-kiyamiyya when defective coins are received.

Core Idea
Defective coins give the buyer khiyar al-kiyamiyya, not khiyar al-ru'yah, because coins are fungible.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification of dirhams and the buyer's rights. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq distinguishes four types of silver coins and clarifies that a buyer who receives defective coins (ziyuf) has a specific option to return them—not the general option of sight, but the option of quantity/quality. This distinction is crucial for understanding commercial transactions involving currency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four dirham types
List the four types: jiyad, ziyuf, nabharjah, and stooqa. Understand their basic definitions as given in the passage.
Jiyad are pure silver coins; ziyuf are rejected by treasury but accepted by merchants; nabharjah are coins merchants reject; stooqa are brass coins colored as silver.
2
Understand the key definitions
Explore each type's level of acceptance in trade and treasury. Note that ziyuf are acceptable in trade if the seller discloses the defect.
The seller must inform the buyer that the coins are ziyuf.
3
Determine the buyer's right
When the buyer receives ziyuf (or finds them after sale), he has the option to return them and demand jiyad. This is called khiyar al-kiyamiyya.
This option is due to a qualitative deficiency in the agreed-upon coins.
4
Distinguish from khiyar al-ru'yah
The passage explicitly states that khiyar al-ru'yah does not apply to coins because they are fungible. Seeing one is like seeing all.
Khiyar al-ru'yah is an option based on sight, not applicable to interchangeable goods.
5
Apply the rule
Given a scenario involving payment with dirhams, state the applicable option and justify your answer using the passage.
Example: A buyer pays for goods with dirhams and later finds they are ziyuf; he can return them under khiyar al-kiyamiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فوجدها زيوفاً في الظهيرية الدراهم أنواع أربعة : جياد ونبهرجة وزيوف وستوقة. واختلفوا في تفسير النبهرجة قال بعضهم : هي التي تضرب في غير دار السلطان . الدراهم المغشوشة والستوقة صفر سموه بالفضة. وقال عامة المشايخ : الجياد فضة خالصة تروج في التجارات وتوضع في بيت المال والزيوف ما يرده بيت المال ولكن تأخذه التجار في التجارات لا بأس بالشراء بها لكن يبين البائع أنها زيوف والنبهرجة ما برجه التجار أي رده . والزيوف هي فكان له الخيار وهذا يسمى خيار الكمية لا خيار الرؤية لأن خيار الرؤية لا يثبت في النقود اهـ.
He said: (then he found them ziyuf) In al-Zahiriyyah: Dirhams are four types: jiyad, nabharjah, ziyuf, and stooqa. They differed in interpretation of nabharjah. Some said: it is those struck outside the authority of the Sultan. The dirhams are adulterated and stooqa is brass they named as silver. The generality of the mashayikh said: jiyad are pure silver, circulated in trade and placed in the treasury; ziyuf are what the treasury rejects but merchants take in trade; there is no harm buying with them but the seller must state they are ziyuf; and nabharjah is what the merchants have rejected, i.e., its rejection. And ziyuf are... Then he has the option, and this is called khiyar al-kiyamiyya (option of quantity) not khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) because khiyar al-ru'yah is not established for coins.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the classification of dirhams into four categories according to al-Zahiriyyah. Jiyad are pure silver coins used in trade and treasury. Ziyuf are low-quality coins that the treasury rejects but merchants accept; the seller must inform the buyer. Nabharjah are coins that merchants have rejected (possibly due to poor minting). Stooqa are brass coins plated or colored like silver. The ruling: if a buyer receives dirhams and finds them to be ziyuf, he has the option to return them and demand jiyad. This option is called khiyar al-kiyamiyya (option of quantity/quality), not khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight), because khiyar al-ru'yah does not apply to currency — since coins are fungible, seeing one is like seeing all. The right to return stems from a qualitative defect in the agreed upon performance, not from failure to inspect.

Key Terms
جياد (jiyad): Pure silver dirhams that are accepted in trade and by the treasury.
زيوف (ziyuf): Defective dirhams that the treasury rejects but merchants may accept; the seller must disclose.
نبهرجة (nabharjah): Dirhams that merchants have rejected (possibly counterfeit or of poor quality).
ستوقة (stooqa): Brass coins plated or colored with silver; they have no legal status as dirhams.
خيار الكمية (khiyar al-kiyamiyya): The option to return a defective item due to a quantitative/qualitative deficiency in the agreed specifications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the name of the option a buyer has when he receives ziyuf dirhams instead of jiyad?
A) Khiyar al-ru'yah
B) Khiyar al-′ayb
C) Khiyar al-kiyamiyya
D) Khiyar al-majlis

Key Takeaway
When a buyer receives defective dirhams (ziyuf) instead of pure ones (jiyad), he has the option to return them under khiyar al-kiyamiyya, which is distinct from khiyar al-ru'yah because coins are fungible.

Classification of Counter-Values in Sale
Taxonomy of counter-values: prices and commodities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any counter-value ('iwad) in a sale as price (thaman) or sold object (mabi') according to Hanafi rules and identify exceptions like salam and manufactured goods.

Core Idea
Gold and silver are always price; unique goods are always sold; fungibles depend on description or specificity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a foundational taxonomy of the two counter-values in a sale. Understanding whether an item functions as price or as the object sold is crucial for determining the validity of sale contracts. The Hanafi school has clear rules: currencies are always price, qimiyyat are always sold, and mithliyat switch roles based on specification. Let's unpack this systematic classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Carefully read the opening sentence: the counter-values are either dirhams/dinars, qimiyyat, or mithliyat. Recognize that the first two (currencies) are always price, the third (qimiyyat) is always sold object.
2
Understand the rule for mithliyat
Mithliyat (fungible goods like wheat, eggs) can be either price or sold object. If described in the dhimma (debt), they are price; if specific (mu'ayyan), they are sold object. This mirrors the salam contract exception.
When both counter-values are mithli and specific, the preposition 'ba' or 'ala' determines which is price.
3
Apply the exception: salam and manufactured goods
Qimiyyat cannot be sold as a debt except when allowed in salam for items like garments. Manufactured gold/silver (utensils) are qimiyyat and become specific by description. Fulus (copper coins) follow the same rule as gold/silver.
4
Note the intra-madhhab difference
Imam Khwahar Zadeh held that when a mithli is exchanged for a qimi, the mithli is price (like currencies), because currencies do not become specific even when designated. This is a minority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الأعواض في البيع إما دراهم أو دنانير أو أعيان قيمية أو مثلية . فالأول والثاني ثمن سواء قوبلت بجنسها أو بغيرها، والثالث مبيعة أبداً ولا يجوز البيع فيها إلا عيناً إلا فيما يجوز السلم فيه كالثياب، وكما ثبت مبيعاً في الذمة سلماً يثبت ديناً مؤجلاً في الذمة على أنها سلم، وحينئذ يشترط الأجل لأنها ثمن بل لكونها ملحقة بالسلم في كونها في ديناً الذمة فلذا قلنا : إذا باع عبداً بثوب موصوف في الذمة إلى أجل جاز ويكون بيعاً في في حق العبد حتى لا يشترط قبضه في المجلس بخلاف ما لو أسلم الدراهم في الثوب. وإنما ظهرت أحكام المسلم فيه في الثوب حتى شرط فيه الأجل وامتنع بيعه قبل قبضه لإلحاقه بالمسلم فيه. والرابع كيلي أو وزني أو عددي متقارب كالبيض فإن قوبلت بالنقود فهي مبيعات أو بأمثالها من المثليات فما كان موصوفاً في الذمة فهو ثمن، وما كان معيناً فمبيع، فإن كان كل منهما معيناً فما صحبه حرف الباء أو «على» كان ثمناً والآخر بيعاً، كذا في فتح القدير وغيره. والفلوس كالنقدين كما في المعراج، ودخل المصوغ من الذهب والفضة كالآنية تحت القيميات فتتعين بالتعيين للصفة، وأما المثلي إذا قوبل بقيمي فلم يدخل فيما ذكرنا. وقال الإمام خواهر زاده: إنه ثمن ومن حكم النقود أنها لا تتعين ولو عينت في عقود المعاوضات
Then know that the counter-values in sale are either dirhams or dinars or specific goods (qimiy) or fungible goods (mithli). The first and second are always price (thaman), whether they are exchanged for their own kind or not. The third is always the sold object and it is not permissible to sell it except as a specific object, except what is permitted for salam like garments. And just as it is established as a sold object in the dhimma by salam, so it becomes a debt payable in the dhimma as if it were a salam. Then the term is required because it is a price, rather because it is attached to salam in being a debt. Hence we say: If someone sells a slave for a garment described in the dhimma with a term, it is valid and it is a sale with respect to the slave so that its receipt is not required in the session, contrary to if he pays dirhams as salam for the garment. The rulings of the muslam fihi appear for the garment so that the term is required and its sale before receipt is prevented because it is treated like muslam fihi. The fourth type is measured, weighed, or counted with close similarity like eggs. If exchanged for currency, they are sold objects; if exchanged for their like from mithliyat, then what is described in the dhimma is price, and what is specific is sold object. If both are specific, then whichever is accompanied by the letter ba or 'ala is the price, and the other is the sold object. This is from Fath al-Qadir and others. Coins (fulus) are like the two currencies as in al-Mi'raj. Jewelry made of gold and silver like utensils enter under qimiyyat, so they become specific by specification of description. As for mithli if exchanged for a qimi, it does not enter under what we have mentioned. Imam Khwahar Zadeh said: It is price, and from the ruling of currencies is that they do not become specific, even if designated in commutative contracts.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a systematic classification of the two counter-values ('iwad) in a sale contract. There are four categories: gold dinars, silver dirhams, qimiyyat (unique goods like a specific cow or house), and mithliyat (fungible goods like wheat, eggs). Gold and silver are always price (thaman) regardless of what they are traded for. Qimiyyat are always the sold object (mabi'); they cannot be sold on credit (in the dhimma) except through a salam contract for permitted items like garments. Mithliyat can be either price or sold object: if described in the dhimma (like a debt of wheat), they function as price; if specified by sight, they are sold object. When both counter-values are specific (mu'ayyan), the letter ba (with) or 'ala (upon) indicates which is the price. Fulus (copper coins) follow the same rule as gold and silver. Manufactured gold and silver objects (like utensils) are qimiyyat and become specific upon description, so they must be identified by sight. When a mithli is exchanged for a qimi, the mithli is considered price per Imam Khwahar Zadeh, and currencies do not become specific even if designated.

Key Terms
أعواض (a'wad): Counter-values in a sale; the two things exchanged (price and sold object).
ثمن (thaman): The price; gold/silver dirhams/dinars always serve as price regardless of specification.
مبيع (mabi'): The sold object; qimiyyat are always mabi'.
قيمي (qimi): Goods that are not fungible, having unique value; e.g., a specific slave, a house.
مثلي (mithli): Goods that are fungible, measured, weighed, or counted with close similarity; e.g., wheat, eggs.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid immediately and the goods are delivered later; only allowed for mithliyat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if both counter-values in a sale are specific (mu'ayyan), how is it determined which one is the price?
A) The party who speaks first determines the price.
B) The object described in more detail is the price.
C) The one preceded by the letter ba or 'ala is the price.
D) The object of lesser value is the price.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, gold/silver are always price, unique goods (qimiyyat) are always sold object, and fungibles (mithliyat) can switch roles depending on specificity or debt description.

Exception for Borrowed Zuyuf Dirhams: Qard as a Trust
Expands an exception to a ruling on zuyuf dirhams in loans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when borrowed dirhams are found zuyuf, explain the legal reasoning that qard is like ariyah, and distinguish the case of sutqah coins.

Core Idea
A loan (qard) is a trust, not a guarantee — defects in borrowed coins give no recourse.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on a ruling in Fath al-Qadir. It presents an exception: ordinarily if a buyer finds the dirhams paid are zuyuf, he can demand good ones. But when those dirhams are borrowed, the borrower has no claim because the loan is treated as a trust (ariyah). The ruling changes if the coins are sutqah (plated coins), which are value-based items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The borrower takes possession of dirhams (qard), then uses dinars to purchase a debt in the session. Later the borrowed dirhams are found to be zuyuf or nahrjah.
The purchase is valid because the price (dinars) is paid in the session.
2
State the general rule
In a sale, if the buyer receives zuyuf dirhams, he normally has recourse to demand good dirhams.
This is the principle from Fath al-Qadir that is being excepted.
3
Identify the exception
Here, no recourse exists for the borrower because the transaction is a loan (qard), not a sale.
The passage says: 'فإنه لا رجوع له بشيء لأن القرض عارية'
4
Understand the proof
Qard is analogized to ariyah (loan for use), which is a trust (amānah) without liability for defects. Thus the lender is not a guarantor of the coins' quality.
The maxim 'القرض عارية' is a Hanafi principle.
5
Contrast with sutqah
If the borrowed coins are sutqah (plated), borrowing them is invalid because sutqah is not a fungible (mithli) but a value-based item (qimiy). The borrower must return the sutqah coins, and if the session has ended, he pays the dinar equivalent.
The ruling differs because sutqah cannot be validly borrowed as a fungible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستثنى من قوله في فتح القدير إذا وجد الدراهم زيوفاً مسألة هي ما إذا استقرض دراهم وقبضها ثم اشترى ما في ذمته بدنانير مقبوضة في المجلس حتى صح ثم وجد دراهم القرض زيوفا أو نبهرجة فإنه لا رجوع له بشيء لأن القرض عارية وهو ينافي الضمان، وإن وجدها ستوقة ردها على المقرض لعدم صحة استقراضها لكونها من القيميات فيرجع بالجياد إن ردها قبل التفرق عن المجلس، وإن كان بعد تفرقهما يرجع بديناره لبطلان الصرف وتمامه في تلخيص الجامع في باب بيع القروض. قال في أوله جاز شراء ما عليه لا ما استقرض عكس المقرض الخ . ثم اعلم أن الأعواض في البيع إما دراهم أو دنانير أو أعيان قيمية أو مثلية . فالأول والثاني ثمن سواء قوبلت بجنسها أو بغيرها، والثالث مبيعة أبداً ولا يجوز البيع فيها إلا عيناً إلا فيما يجوز السلم فيه كالثياب، وكما ثبت مبيعاً في الذمة سلماً يثبت ديناً مؤجلاً الذمة على أنها سلم، وحينئذ يشترط الأجل لأنها ثمن بل لكونها ملحقة بالسلم في كونها في ديناً الذمة فلذا قلنا : إذا باع عبداً بثوب موصوف في الذمة إلى أجل جاز ويكون بيعاً في في حق العبد حتى لا يشترط قبضه في المجلس بخلاف ما لو أسلم الدراهم في الثوب. وإنما ظهرت أحكام المسلم فيه في الثوب حتى شرط فيه الأجل وامتنع بيعه قبل قبضه لإلحاقه بالمسلم فيه. والرابع كيلي أو وزني أو عددي متقارب كالبيض فإن قوبلت بالنقود فهي مبيعات أو بأمثالها من المثليات فما كان موصوفاً في الذمة فهو ثمن، وما كان معيناً فمبيع، فإن كان كل منهما معيناً فما صحبه حرف الباء أو «على» كان ثمناً والآخر بيعاً، كذا في فتح القدير وغيره. والفلوس كالنقدين كما في المعراج، ودخل المصوغ من الذهب والفضة كالآنية تحت القيميات فتتعين بالتعيين للصفة، وأما المثلي إذا قوبل بقيمي فلم يدخل فيما ذكرنا. وقال الإمام خواهر زاده: إنه ثمن ومن حكم النقود أنها لا تتعين ولو عينت في عقود المعاوضات
It is excepted from his statement in Fath al-Qadir: 'If he finds the dirhams zuyuf' – a case: when he borrowed dirhams and took possession of them, then he purchased what was owed to him with dinars taken in the session so that it is valid, then he finds the borrowed dirhams zuyuf or nahrjah – then he has no recourse to anything because a loan is a loan [ariyah] and that negates guarantee. If he finds them sutqah, he returns them to the lender because borrowing them is not valid since they are of the value-based category (qimiyyat), so he demands the good [dirhams] if he returned them before parting from the session. If it was after their parting, he demands his dinar because the currency exchange is void. Its completion is in Talkhis al-Jami' in the chapter on sale of loans. He said at its beginning: 'It is permissible to purchase what he owes, not what he borrowed; opposite of the lender.' Then know that the countervalues in sale are either dirhams or dinars, or specific value-based or fungible items. The first and second are price whether exchanged for like or other. The third is always the sold item, and sale therein is not allowed except as a specific item except in what salam is allowed in like garments. And just as it is established as a sold item in a debt via salam, it is established as a deferred debt in the dhimmah with it being a sale [as a price]...
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling from Fath al-Qadir, providing an exception to the general rule on finding zuyuf (counterfeit) dirhams. The general rule: if a buyer finds the dirhams paid are zuyuf, he can return them and demand good dirhams. But here, the dirhams are borrowed (qard) and then used as payment for a purchase of a debt with dinars. The legal reasoning: qard is a form of ariyah (loan for use), which does not carry liability for defects (damān). Thus if the borrowed dirhams turn out zuyuf, the borrower has no claim against the lender. However, if they are sutqah (a coin with silver on both sides but copper inside), the borrowing is invalid because such coins are treated as value-based (qimiy) rather than fungible (mithli), so the lender is in possession of a trust and must return the actual coins if not commingled. The commentator then explains the different categories of countervalues in sale, discussing when they are treated as price (thaman) or sold item (mabi'), the role of specification (ta'yin), and the rulings for fulus (copper coins) and gold/silver wares. He quotes Imam Khawahrzada that currency is not specified even if designated in commutative contracts, except in cases of usurpation and trusts.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyūf): Counterfeit dirhams that are not pure silver but mixed or base
ستوقة (sutqah): A coin with silver on both sides and copper in between, treated as a value-based item (qimiy) not fungible
عارية (āriyah): A loan for use without compensation, treated as a trust (amānah) without liability for loss or defect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone borrows dirhams and later finds they are zuyuf, what is the ruling?
A) The borrower can demand good dirhams from the lender.
B) The borrower has no recourse because the loan is like a trust.
C) The borrower must return the zuyuf dirhams immediately.
D) The purchase is void.

Key Takeaway
A loan (qard) is treated as a trust (ariyah) in Hanafi law, so the lender is not liable for defects like zuyuf; only sutqah coins trigger a different ruling due to their value-based nature.

Classification of Countervalues in Sales: Thaman vs Mabi'
Classifies countervalues in sales and sets rules for price vs sold object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize countervalues in a sale (thaman, mabi', value-based, fungible) and apply the rules for when each can serve as price or sold item.

Core Idea
In Hanafi sales law, the countervalues are either price (thaman) or sold object (mabi') depending on their nature and how they are specified.

After discussing the exception on borrowed zuyuf, the commentator expands on the general principles governing countervalues in sales. He explains that dirhams and dinars are always price, while specific items are sold objects. Fungible items can be both depending on whether they are described in the dhimma or specified.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the four categories
The countervalues are either dirhams/dinars (thaman), value-based specific items (qimiyyat), or fungible items (mithliyyat). Dirhams and dinars are always price, whether exchanged for their like or other.
The first two are always price; the third is always sold item.
2
Apply the rule for value-based items
Value-based items (e.g., a specific shirt) are always the sold object (mabi') and cannot be sold as a price except in salam contracts (like garments).
Exception: salam allows selling described fungibles in dhimma as price.
3
Understand the effect of specification (tayin)
If a fungible is described in the dhimma (debt), it serves as price; if specified (mu'ayyan), it is the sold object. If both are specified, the one accompanied by the preposition 'ba' or 'ala' is price.
This rule clarifies ambiguous transactions.
4
Note special cases
Fulus (copper coins) are treated like dirhams/dinars. Gold and silver wares (e.g., utensils) are considered value-based (qimiy), so they are subject to specification.
Also, a fungible paired with a value-based item: Khawahrzada said it becomes thaman, and currency objects do not become specified even if designated in commutative contracts.
5
Consider the principle that currency is not specified
Imam Khawahrzada states that currency (dirhams/dinars) are never specified even if explicitly named in a contract, except in cases of usurpation and trusts.
This preserves the fungibility of currency.

This passage is an expansion of a ruling from Fath al-Qadir, providing an exception to the general rule on finding zuyuf (counterfeit) dirhams. The general rule: if a buyer finds the dirhams paid are zuyuf, he can return them and demand good dirhams. Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale; it is either dirhams, dinars, or another fungible described in the dhimma.
مبيع (mabi'): The sold object; it is always a specific item or a value-based item.
قيمي (qimiy): A value-based item that is not fungible; its value is determined by its unique qualities.
مثلي (mithli): A fungible item that has counterparts of similar quality; it can be replaced by similar items.
فلوس (fulus): Copper coins that are treated like dirhams and dinars in being price.

B) The item with the lower value is the price.

D) The item mentioned first is the price.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales, dirhams and dinars are always price; value-based items are always sold objects; fungibles are price if described in dhimma, sold object if specific; and the preposition 'ba' or 'ala' decides when both are specific.

Determination of Specific Objects in Contracts (Ta'ayyun al-'Ayn)
Ruling on ta'ayyun in various contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state for each contract type (ghasb, amanat, wakala, mahr, void compensatory, kitaba, manumission conditional upon payment) whether the specific object is determined (ta'ayyun) and explain the general rule for rescission in entitlement.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, ta'ayyun (determination of a specific object) depends on the contract type: non-compensatory contracts determine the object; compensatory contracts generally do not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out a key distinction in Hanafi mu'amalat: the concept of ta'ayyun—when a specific object becomes legally fixed and must be returned in kind. The ruling varies based on whether the contract is compensatory or not, and whether it involves entitlement (istihqaq). By learning these categories, you will understand how to determine the obligation of returning the exact object versus its equivalent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for rescission in entitlement
The passage begins with the rule that in rescission due to entitlement (istihqaq), the specific object is not claimed; the buyer may keep it and pay its equivalent in quantity and description.
This is the default for sale rescission.
2
List contracts where ta'ayyun applies
The passage states that ta'ayyun applies in ghasb (usurpation), amanat (trusts), wakala (agency), and in every non-compensatory contract.
These are non-compensatory relationships.
3
List contracts where ta'ayyun does not apply
Ta'ayyun does not apply in mahr (dowry) before divorce and after divorce before consummation, and in kitaba (contract of manumission). Void compensatory contracts have two narrations.
Mahr is a compensatory contract.
4
Identify the exception in manumission conditional upon payment
Manumission conditional upon payment (al-'itq al-mu'allaq bi al-ada') is treated differently: ta'ayyun applies.
This is despite being a form of compensation.
5
Recognize the two narrations in void compensatory contracts
The passage mentions two narrations (riwayatan) regarding ta'ayyun in void compensatory contracts, indicating an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
This means the ruling is not settled in that category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع وفسوخها في حق الاستحقاق فلا يستحق عينها فللمشتري إمساكها ودفع مثلها قدراً ووصفاً. ويتعينان في الغصوب والأمانات والوكالات على تفصيل فيها، وكذا في كل عقد ليس معاوضة، ولا يتعين في المهر قبل الطلاق وبعده قبل الدخول وفي تعيينها في المعاوضات الفاسدة روايتان ولا تتعين في الكتابة وتتعين في العتق المعلق بالأداء. والفرق بينهما في الظهيرية من المكاتب وتمامه فيما كتبناه من القواعد الفقهية
The book of sale: and its rescissions in the right of entitlement: the specific object is not entitled; thus the buyer may keep it and pay its equivalent in quantity and description. And they (the two objects) are determined in usurpations, trusts, and agencies with detail therein, and likewise in every contract that is not compensatory. And it is not determined in the dowry before divorce and after it before consummation. And regarding their determination in void compensatory contracts, there are two narrations. And it is not determined in kitaba (contract of manumission) and is determined in manumission conditional upon payment. The difference between them is in al-Zahiriya from al-Mukatib, and its completion is in what we wrote in al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the book of sale. It discusses the legal concept of ta'ayyun (determination of a specific object) in different types of contracts. The general rule is that in contracts of rescission (فسوخ) regarding entitlement (استحقاق), the specific object itself is not claimed; rather, the buyer may keep it and pay its equivalent in quantity and description. However, in cases of usurpation (غصب), trusts (أمانات), and agency (وكالات), the specific object is determined and must be returned in kind. The same applies to any non-compensatory contract. An exception is the dowry (مهر): it is not determined before divorce or after it before consummation. For void compensatory contracts (معاوضات فاسدة), there are two narrations: one says it is determined, the other not. In kitaba (contract of manumission), the object is not determined, but in manumission conditional upon payment (عتق معلق بالأداء), it is determined. The passage ends by referring to a fuller explanation in al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya.

Key Terms
فسوخ (fusukh): Rescissions of contracts, particularly in the context of entitlement (istihqaq).
استحقاق (istihqaq): Entitlement: when a third party proves ownership of the sold object.
تعيين (ta'ayyun): Determination: the specific object becomes legally fixed and must be returned in kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, in which of the following is the specific object NOT determined (la yata'ayyan)?
A) Ghasb (usurpation)
B) Kitaba (contract of manumission)
C) Void compensatory contracts (according to one narration)
D) Manumission conditional upon payment

Key Takeaway
The determination of a specific object (ta'ayyun) in Hanafi fiqh depends on the contract type: non-compensatory contracts require return of the exact object; compensatory contracts generally require only its equivalent, with the exception of manumission conditional upon payment.

Liability During Weighing in Sale
Ruling on liability for loss during weighing when the buyer uses the seller's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for loss during weighing in a sale and identify the key condition (permission) that determines who bears the loss.

Core Idea
Permission determines liability: weighing with permission transfers risk; without permission, the weigher bears the loss.

In Hanafi fiqh, the transfer of possession in a sale can occur through weighing, but the liability for any loss during the process depends on who authorized the weighing. This lesson examines a classic case from al-Qunyah where a person gives money to a grocer to buy an item, and some of the goods are lost while being weighed. The ruling hinges on whether the grocer weighed with the buyer's permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the action
The scenario involves a payer (the buyer) and a grocer (the seller). The grocer weighs the item to determine the quantity. During this weighing, some of the goods are lost.
2
Isolate the variable: permission
The key factor is whether the grocer weighed with the payer's permission. The ruling splits into two branches.
Permission is explicitly mentioned in the source as 'bi-idhni al-dafi'.
3
Apply the conditional ruling
If permission was given, the loss is from the payer's wealth; if not, the loss is from the grocer's wealth.
This is the direct ruling from the text.
4
Understand the rationale
With permission, the weighing is considered an act of taking possession by the payer, so the risk passes to the payer. Without permission, the grocer is still responsible for the goods as he acted without authority.
This illustrates the principle that possession (qabd) can occur through weighing with consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفع إلى بقال ثمناً ليشتري به شيئاً فوزنه فضاع منه شيء قبل الفراغ منه، فإن وزنه بأذن الدافع ضاع من مال الدافع، وما وزنه ضاع من مال البقال
He paid to a grocer a price to buy something with it, then he weighed it, and something was lost from it before finishing. If he weighed it with the permission of the payer, it is lost from the wealth of the payer; and what he weighed is lost from the wealth of the grocer.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This is a ruling from al-Qunyah regarding a specific case: a person gives money to a grocer to buy something. The grocer weighs the item, and some part is lost during the weighing process. The ruling depends on permission: if the grocer weighed with the payer's permission (meaning the payer authorized him to weigh and take possession), then any loss is borne by the payer (the principal). If the grocer weighed without permission, the loss is from the grocer's own wealth, as he is responsible for the goods until delivery to the payer. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of daman (liability) in sales and agency.

Key Terms
وزن (wazn): The act of weighing, which is a mode of taking possession in sale.
دافع (dafi'): The person who gives money to another to purchase goods; the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer gives money to a seller to weigh an item and the seller weighs without permission, and a loss occurs, who bears the loss?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Both equally
D) The original owner

Key Takeaway
Permission is decisive: weighing with permission transfers loss to the buyer; without permission, the seller bears the loss.

Specifying Price Quality in a Sale
Ruling on price quality specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on specifying the quality of a price paid in kind and identify the condition for a valid sale.

Core Idea
A sale is invalid if the price quality is unspecified — certainty in price is a condition.

When paying with a commodity like hanatha, the quality must be specified (good, medium, or poor) to avoid uncertainty (gharar). This lesson explains why this is a condition and the consequence of omitting it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commodity used as price
Recognize that 'hanatha' is a type of grain or coin used as currency in this transaction.
The price medium must be identified first.
2
Determine the required specification
The seller must clarify whether the hanatha is good (جيدة), medium (وسط), or poor (رديئة).
These categories cover the range of quality.
3
State the ruling
If quality is left unspecified, the sale is invalid because the price is not fully known.
The text uses 'لا يصح' indicating the contract is fasid (defective).
4
Understand the consequence
The invalidity arises from gharar in the price, making the contract fasid until specification is provided.
Hanafi distinguishes fasid from batil; fasid can be cured by removing the defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشراء بالحنظة لا يصح ما لم يبين أنها جيدة أو وسط أو رديئة. بعتك عبدي بمنافع دارك سنة لا يجوز . ثم رقم هذا بيع في حق العبد إجارة في حق الدار فإنه جائز .
Buying with hanatha is not valid unless it is clarified whether it is good, medium, or poor. I sell you my slave for the benefits of your house for one year: it is not permissible. Then symbol: this is a sale regarding the slave and a hire regarding the house, so it is permissible.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First: if the price is paid in a specific commodity like hanatha (a type of grain or coin), the quality must be specified (good, medium, or inferior) otherwise the sale is invalid. This is because the price must be known to avoid gharar (uncertainty). Second: a single contract that combines sale (of a slave) and hire (of a house's usufruct) is initially impermissible because it mixes two different types of contracts. However, by analyzing the transaction, it is actually a sale regarding the slave and a hire regarding the house, so each part is considered separately and the contract becomes permissible. This shows the Hanafi approach of breaking down composite transactions.

Key Terms
حنظة (hanatha): A type of grain or coin used as currency, needing quality specification.
جيدة (jayyidah): Good quality, one of the three required specifications.
وسط (wast): Medium quality, one of the three grades.
رديئة (radiy'ah): Poor quality, the lowest grade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sale invalid if the price quality is not specified?
A) Because the price becomes unknown
B) Because the seller might cheat
C) Because the buyer might not pay
D) Because the commodity is invalid

Key Takeaway
Price certainty includes quality specification; otherwise the sale is fasid.

Combining Sale and Hire in One Contract
Ruling on mixed sale and hire

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a composite contract and determine its validity by separating its components.

Core Idea
A mixture of sale and hire is permissible when each part is treated as a separate valid contract.

A single contract that exchanges a slave for the usufruct of a house for a year seems invalid because it mixes sale and hire. However, Hanafi jurists analyze it as two contracts: a sale of the slave and a hire of the house, each valid. This lesson teaches you to parse such mixed contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
The contract exchanges a slave (sale) for the benefits of a house for one year (hire).
Recognize that the consideration is the usufruct, not the house itself.
2
State the initial ruling
The passage says 'لا يجوز' (not permissible) because the contract mixes two different contract types.
Mixing sale and hire in one contract is generally forbidden.
3
Apply the analytical method
The text uses 'ثم رقم' to indicate a breakdown: it is a sale regarding the slave and a hire regarding the house.
Separate the contract into its components.
4
Evaluate each component
Each component is independently valid: the sale of the slave and the hire of the house's benefits both meet their respective conditions.
Both contracts are sahih on their own.
5
Conclude the final ruling
Therefore, the composite contract is permissible because it is really two valid contracts.
The overall contract becomes جائز (permissible).

Source Text

Buying with hanatha is not valid unless it is clarified whether it is good, medium, or poor. I sell you my slave for the benefits of your house for one year: it is not permissible. Then symbol: this is a sale regarding the slave and a hire regarding the house, so it is permissible.

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First: if the price is paid in a specific commodity like hanatha (a type of grain or coin), the quality must be specified (good, medium, or inferior) otherwise the sale is invalid. This is because the price must be known to avoid gharar (uncertainty). Second: a single contract that combines sale (of a slave) and hire (of a house's usufruct) is initially impermissible because it mixes two different types of contracts. However, by analyzing the transaction, it is actually a sale regarding the slave and a hire regarding the house, so each part is considered separately and the contract becomes permissible. This shows the Hanafi approach of breaking down composite transactions.

Recovery After Void Sale with Partial Repayment
Ruling on a buyer's right to recover the remaining price after a void sale and a subsequent small purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a seller can reclaim the remaining price after a void sale when he has used part of it to buy a trifle from the buyer.

Core Idea
The price already spent in a separate purchase from the buyer is not recoverable, even after a void sale.

When a void sale is rescinded, parties normally return what they received. But what if the seller has already spent part of the price on a small purchase from the same buyer? This lesson examines a precise scenario from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq where the seller cannot reclaim that portion because it was consumed in a new, independent transaction. Understanding this exception helps you grasp how Hanafi sales law balances rescission rights with transactional finality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the original sale
The seller sold land for forty dirhams. The buyer paid only thirty-five. The remaining five was not handed over.
The original sale is void (or later voided by defect/option).
2
Identify the second transaction
The seller used the unpaid five to buy a trifling item from the same buyer. This is a separate purchase.
The item is described as 'muhaqqar' – insignificant in value.
3
Apply the rescission of the original sale
Because the original sale is void, the parties must return whatever they received. The seller received thirty-five, so he returns the land. He also must return the five?
The text says he cannot demand the five back from the buyer.
4
Understand why the five is not returned
The seller voluntarily used that five to buy the trifle from the buyer. That second sale is valid and independent. To recover the five, the seller would have to unwind that second sale, which the law does not require because the buyer has already parted with the trifle.
The ruling protects the buyer's possession of the five via the second transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باع ضيعة بأربعين فقبض خمسة وثلاثين واشترى بالخمسة الباقية من المشتري شيئاً محقراً قيمته قليلة ثم تبين بطلان البيع أو ردها المشتري بعيب أو شرط أو خيار ليس له أن يطلب الخمسة التي باع ذلك الشيء بها.
He sold a land for forty, and he received thirty-five, and he bought from the buyer with the remaining five a trifling thing of little value. Then it becomes clear that the sale is void, or the buyer returns it due to a defect, condition, or option. He has no right to demand the five for which he sold that thing.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This ruling presents a scenario: a seller sells land for forty, but only receives thirty-five. He then uses the remaining five to buy a small, inexpensive item from the same buyer. Later the original sale is discovered void, or the buyer exercises a right to return (due to defect, option, etc.). The seller cannot reclaim the five from the buyer because that five was used in a separate transaction (purchase of the trifle). The principle is that when a void sale is rescinded, the parties must return what they received. However, if the seller has already spent part of the price in a purchase from the buyer, that part is not recoverable as it is considered a new, independent contract. This protects the integrity of sequential transactions.

Key Terms
محقر (muhaqqar): Something insignificant, small in value relative to the transaction or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the void sale never occurred
D) Because the seller waived the right

Key Takeaway
After a void sale, the seller cannot reclaim the part of the price that he spent on a small purchase from the buyer, because that amount is now governed by a separate valid sale.

Defective Coins Treated as Good in Six Cases
Enumeration of six cases where bad coins equal good coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six cases where defective coins are treated as good coins in Hanafi fiqh and explain the ruling for each.

Core Idea
In specific transactions, paying with bad coins fulfills an obligation measured by good coins.

This passage from al-Walwaljiya enumerates six legal scenarios where defective coins (zuyuf) carry the same legal weight as good coins (jiyad). Understanding these cases is essential for correctly applying currency rulings in sales, suretyship, oaths, and debts. The principle is that once bad coins are accepted or spent, they satisfy the underlying obligation measured by good coins.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the general principle
Recognize that in these six cases, defective coins (zuyuf) are legally equivalent to good coins (jiyad). This means obligations measured in good coins can be fulfilled by paying bad coins, and the legal effects follow accordingly.
This is an exception to the general rule that bad coins are not equivalent; it applies only in these specific contexts.
2
Memorize the first five cases from al-Walwaljiya
Memorize each of the five cases: (1) preemption, (2) surety, (3) murabaha, (4) oath, (5) debt discharge. For each, understand the specific ruling: the preemptor pays with good coins, surety recovers good coins, capital in murabaha is good coins, oath not broken, creditor cannot later demand good coins after spending bad ones.
Use mnemonics: the first letters spell P-S-M-O-D (Preemption, Surety, Murabaha, Oath, Debt).
3
Understand the sixth added case
The sixth case from Talkhis al-Jami' involves a loan repayment: borrowed dirhams are treated like good dirhams when returned, even if later found defective.
This expands the principle to loan transactions.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute in case 5
In case 5, Abu Yusuf disagreed with Muhammad and Abu Yusuf (the two). According to the two, the creditor cannot recourse for good coins after spending bad ones. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that of the two.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the cases to similar scenarios
Test your understanding by creating hypotheticals. For example, if someone owed 100 good dirhams but paid 100 bad dirhams that were accepted and spent, does the oath break? Answer: No (case 4).
Practice with variations to solidify the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزاد في الزيوف بقدر شعيرة[؟] يدخل بين الوزنين لا يجوز .اهـ. وفي الولوالجية من الشفعة : الزيوف من الدراهم بمنزلة الجياد في خمس مسائل : الأولى مسألة الشفعة إذا اشترى بالجياد ونقد الزيوف أخذ الشفيع بالجياد الثانية الكفيل إذا كفل بالجياد ونقد للبائع الزيوف يرجع على المكفول عنه بالجياد الثالثة إذا اشترى شيئاً بالجياد ونقد البائع الزيوف ثم باعه مرابحة فإن رأس المال هو الجياد الرابعة حلف ليقضينه حقه اليوم وكان عليه جياد فقضاه الزيوف لا يحنث . الخامسة له على آخر دراهم جياد فقبض الزيوف وأنفقها فلم يعلم إلا بعد الإنفاق لا يرجع عليه بالجياد في قولهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف اهـ. ويزاد سادسة ما نقلناه عن تلخيص الجامع استقرض دراهم وقبضها ثم اشترى ما في ذمته بدنانير مقبوضة في المجلس ثم وجد دراهم القرض يرجع بشيء ففيها الزيوف كالجياد
And he added regarding defective coins (zuyuf) to the extent of a small measure, entering between the two weighings, it is not permissible. End. And in al-Walwaljiya from the section on preemption: defective dirhams are like good dirhams in five issues: first, the issue of preemption: if he bought with good coins and paid bad coins, the preemptor takes with good coins. Second, the surety: if he guaranteed with good coins and paid the seller bad coins, he recovers from the principal with good coins. Third, if he bought something with good coins and the seller paid bad coins, then sold it as murabaha, the capital is the good coins. Fourth, he swore to pay his right today, and he owed good coins, but he paid bad coins: he does not break his oath. Fifth, he had good dirhams owed by another; he received bad coins and spent them, not knowing until after spending: he does not recourse for good coins according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. End. And a sixth is added from Talkhis al-Jami': he borrowed dirhams and received them, then bought what was owed to him with dinars taken in the session, then he found the dirhams of the loan: he recourses for something, and therein bad coins are like good coins.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage from al-Walwaljiya lists five cases where defective coins (zuyuf) are treated as equivalent to good coins (jiyad) in their legal effect, followed by a sixth added from Talkhis al-Jami'. The principle is that once a transaction is completed with bad coins, they are considered as valid currency for certain purposes. Case 1: In preemption (shuf'a), if the buyer paid with bad coins, the preemptor must take at the price of good coins. Case 2: In suretyship (kafala), the surety who paid bad coins recovers good coins from the principal. Case 3: In murabaha resale, the cost basis is the good coins originally agreed. Case 4: In oaths, paying bad coins fulfills the obligation if good were owed. Case 5: In debt discharge, if the creditor received bad coins and spent them, he cannot later demand good coins from the debtor, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); Abu Yusuf disagrees and allows recourse. The sixth case involves a loan repaid with borrowed dirhams later found to be bad, where they are treated like good. This shows the complexity of currency in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Defective or counterfeit coins, not of standard quality.
جياد (jiyad): Good, genuine coins of full weight and purity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which case is there a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)?
A) Preemption (case 1)
B) Surety (case 2)
C) Debt discharge (case 5)
D) Murabaha (case 3)

Key Takeaway
In six specific legal contexts, defective coins (zuyuf) are treated as equivalent to good coins (jiyad) for fulfilling obligations and determining legal effects.

Equal Division of Price for Indistinguishable Co-Owned Property
Ruling on dividing the price equally when two owners sell indistinguishable slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dividing the sale price of indistinguishable joint property and apply it to similar cases of co-ownership ambiguity.

Core Idea
When ownership is ambiguous, equal division of price rules—shape controls over value.

Sometimes two people jointly own two items (e.g., slaves, houses) and neither knows which specific item belongs to him. If they sell both together, how is the price split? The natural assumption might be proportional to value, but Hanafi law presumes equality. This lesson examines a key ruling from Abu Yusuf that establishes this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
The case involves two owners each having one slave, but the identity of each owner's slave is unknown. They sell both slaves as a set.
The ambiguity is the central fact.
2
Recognize the requirement of permission (ijaza)
One owner sells both slaves with the permission of the other. Without ijaza, the sale would not be valid for the other owner's share.
Ijaza makes the sale legally effective for both shares.
3
Apply the ruling: equal division of price
Even if one slave is more valuable, the price is divided equally between the two owners. The text says 'al-thaman baynahuma nifsan' (the price is half and half).
Equality is based on the number of owners, not the value of their property.
4
Extend to other properties
The same rule applies to houses: only the number of houses matters, not their relative worth.
The principle is general: fungible, indistinguishable assets.
5
Understand the underlying maxim
The ruling reflects the Hanafi principle that in cases of uncertainty about ownership shares, equal division is the default, as no proof of inequality exists.
This avoids injustice and adheres to the maxim 'al-ashabah bi al-tasawiy' (sharing equally).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية عن أبي يوسف : عبدان لرجلين لم يعرف كل واحد منهما عبده من عبد صاحبه فباعهما أحد الموليين بإجازة الآخر وأحدهما أكثر من الآخر، فالثمن بينهما نصفان وكذا البيوت فإنما أنظر إلى عددها لا إلى فضل بعضها على بعض .
And in al-Qunyah from Abu Yusuf: Two slaves belonging to two men, each not knowing his slave from the other's slave, so one of the owners sold them with the permission of the other, and one is more valuable than the other. Then the price is half between them. And similarly houses: I look only to their number, not to the superiority of some over others.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This ruling from Abu Yusuf, cited in al-Qunyah, deals with a joint sale of indistinguishable property by two co-owners. If two slaves are owned by two different persons, and neither knows which slave belongs to him, they may jointly sell them. Even if one slave is more valuable than the other, the price is divided equally between the two owners. The same applies to houses: only the number of properties matters, not their relative value. This is because the ownership is uncertain and each owner's share is presumed equal in the absence of identification. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that when ownership is ambiguous, assets are divided equally.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijaza): Permission from the other owner to sell the jointly owned property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In proportion to their values
B) Equally between the two owners
C) According to the buyer's choice
D) The more valuable slave's owner takes the larger share

Key Takeaway
When jointly owned property is indistinguishable and sold together, the price is split equally among the owners irrespective of value differences.

Validity of Sale with Unmixed Currency in a Bag
Ruling on the validity of a sale when the price is assumed dirhams but found to be dinars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a sale where the price is assumed to be one currency but found to be another, and identify the legal principle that validates the transaction.

Core Idea
A sale is valid even if the actual currency differs from what was assumed, because both currencies are the same genus for monetary value.

In this passage, we encounter a practical scenario in commercial transactions: a buyer agrees to purchase using the contents of a bag he assumes to be dirhams, but later discovers the bag contains dinars. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling that upholds the validity of the sale, relying on the principle that gold and silver currencies are considered a single genus (jins) for purposes such as zakat and debt. This ruling demonstrates the school's flexible yet principled approach to ensuring the stability of contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the parties are doing: the buyer purchases with the contents of a bag assumed to contain dirhams. The ruling is that the sale is valid (sahih).
2
Identify the legal issue
The price was assumed to be dirhams but turned out to be dinars. This creates a potential for uncertainty (gharar) in the price.
3
Determine the Hanafi solution
The sale is valid because both currencies are considered the same genus (jins) in monetary matters, so the price is known through the genus.
4
Apply the consequence
The buyer must pay the equivalent in dirhams of the local currency, as stated in the text.
5
Note the overarching principle
The principle that 'dinar and dirham are of one genus' is used, which also applies in zakat calculations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشترى بما في هذا الكيس من الدراهم فإذا فيه دنانير جاز البيع لأنها جنس في حق الزكاة وعليه ملء هذا الكيس من الدراهم نقد بلده وكذا عند تفاوت النقدين اهـ.
He bought with what is in this bag of dirhams, but it turns out to contain dinars. The sale is valid because they are a genus regarding zakat, and he owes the fill of this bag in dirhams of the currency of his city, and likewise when the two currencies differ. End.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This ruling from al-Qunyah addresses a case where a buyer agrees to buy using the contents of a bag assumed to be dirhams, but the bag actually contains dinars (gold coins). The sale is still valid because both are monetary currencies (thaman) and are considered a single genus for purposes like zakat. However, the buyer must pay dirhams to the value of the bag's contents, in the local currency. If the two currencies differ in value, the obligation is to pay the equivalent in dirhams. This shows the flexibility of Hanafi law regarding the price when there is a mistake about the specific currency but the transaction is still valid because the price is essentially a debt of monetary value.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Genus; dinar and dirham are considered the same genus for zakat and sale purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the buyer agreed to the bag's contents
D) Because the seller allowed it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a sale even when the actual currency differs from the assumed currency, based on the principle that both are of the same monetary genus.

Price in Measurable Goods, Custom, and Broker Liability
Multiple rulings: price in measurable goods, custom in currency, and broker liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on specifying the place of delivery when the price is a measurable debt, apply custom ('urf) to determine the actual price in a sale, and identify when a broker is not liable for loss of the price.

Core Idea
When the price is a measurable debt, specify delivery place; custom governs currency; broker not liable after collecting with permission.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq covers three distinct but interconnected issues regarding the price (thaman) in sales. First, what happens when the price is a measurable or weighable commodity that becomes unavailable? Second, how does local custom interpret the actual value of currency? Third, what is the broker's liability after collecting the price? These rulings illustrate key Hanafi principles on specification, 'urf, and agency.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish specific vs. debt price
Identify the two scenarios: (1) the price is a specific measurable good (e.g., grapes) that later becomes unavailable; (2) the price is a debt of a measurable good (e.g., a kurr of wheat in the dhimmah). The ruling differs: in the first, the sale remains valid; in the second, the place of delivery must be specified.
The text corrects the view that unavailability voids the sale.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on delivery place
When the price is a measurable debt, Abu Hanifa requires specifying the place of delivery; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the contract place suffices. Note that the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
This dispute affects the validity condition.
3
Apply custom to determine price
In the second ruling, if local custom treats five-sixths of a dinar as a dinar, the contract is interpreted according to that custom. This is an application of the Hanafi maxim 'al-‘ādah muḥakkamah' (custom is authoritative).
Custom must be widespread and consistent.
4
Analyze broker liability
If a broker (dallal) collects the price with the owner's permission and then the currency becomes worthless, there is no claim against the buyer. The broker is not liable because he acted as an agent. Also, if a buyer pays extra by mistake and the seller takes it, the seller is not liable for loss of the excess as it was taken as part of the price.
The broker is a trustee (amin) when acting with permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين برقم (قش) لو جعل الكيلي أو الوزني ثمناً بأن جعل العنب مثلاً ثمناً فانقطع يفسد البيع. ثم رقم (ط) قولهم بأنه يفسد بانقطاعه ليس بصحيح فإن من اشترى شيئاً بقفيز رطب في الذمة فانقطع أوانه لا ينتقض البيع، ولو جعل الكيلي أو الوزني الذمة يشترط بيان محل الإيفاء حتى لو باع قناً بكر برّ في الذمة فإنه يشترط بيان محل إيفائه عند أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح، وعندهما يتعين محل العقد للإيفاء وما يصلح ثمناً يصلح أجرة، وما لا يصلح ثمناً يصلح أجرة أيضاً كالأعيان اهـ. وفي التتارخانية معزياً إلى النوازل : سئل والدي عمن باع شيئاً من آخر بعشرة دنانير وقد استقرت العادة في ذلك البلد أنهم يصرفون الأثمان فيما بينهم فيعطون كل خمسة أسداس مكان الدينار واشتهرت تلك العادة هل لبائع ذلك العين أن يطالب المشتري بالوزن أم ينعقد العقد على الذي تعارفه المسلمون فيما بينهم بطريق الدلالة ؟ فقال : ينصرف إلى ما تعارفه الناس فيما بينهم اهـ. وههنا مسائل مناسبة للثمن لا بأس بذكرها تكثيراً للفوائد : لو استوفى الدلال الثمن ثم كسد في يده فلا مطالبة على المشتري حيث باع بإذن المالك، ولو دفع المشتري إلى البائع أكثر من حقه غلطاً...عبارة التتارخانية : رجل باع من آخر شيئاً بألف درهم فوزن له المشتري ألفاً ومائتي درهم فقبضها البائع وضاعت من يده فهو مستوفي الثمن ولا ضمان عليه لأنه بقدر الألف استوفى حصته، وفيما زاد على
And in Jami' al-Fusulayn, entry (qaf-shin): If one makes a measurable or weighable item as a price, e.g., making grapes as a price, and it becomes unavailable, the sale becomes void. Then entry (ta): Their saying that it becomes void by its unavailability is not correct, for if someone buys something with a qafiz of fresh dates as a debt and its season ends, the sale is not rescinded. And if one makes a measurable or weighable item as a debt, it is necessary to specify the place of delivery. So if he sells a slave for a kurr of wheat as a debt, it is necessary to specify the place of delivery according to Abu Hanifa, and it is the correct view. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the place of the contract is determined for delivery. And whatever is suitable as a price is suitable as a hire fee, and whatever is not suitable as a price is yet suitable as a hire fee, like specific objects. End. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya, quoting al-Nawazil: My father was asked about someone who sold something to another for ten dinars, and the custom in that country is to convert prices among themselves, giving five-sixths for a dinar, and this custom is widespread. Is the seller entitled to demand the weight, or does the contract refer to what Muslims customarily understand by way of indication? He replied: It refers to what people customarily understand among themselves. End. And here are issues relevant to the price, it is good to mention them to multiply benefits: If the broker takes the price and then it becomes worthless in his hand, there is no claim against the buyer if he sold with the owner's permission. And if the buyer pays the seller more than his right by mistake... from al-Tatarkhaniyya: A man sold something to another for a thousand dirhams, and the buyer weighed out one thousand two hundred dirhams, the seller took them and they were lost from his hand. He is considered to have received the price and there is no liability on him because he received his due to the extent of a thousand, and what exceeded...
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This final lesson covers three distinct but related issues regarding the price (thaman) in sales. First, a discussion from Jami' al-Fusulayn about when the price is a specific measurable or weighable commodity (e.g., grapes) that later becomes unavailable. There are two views: one says the sale is void, the other (considered correct) says the sale remains valid, citing the example of buying with a qafiz of fresh dates as a debt; the sale does not become void when the season ends. However, if the price is a debt of a measurable commodity, the place of delivery must be specified according to Abu Hanifa (the correct view). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the place of contract determines delivery. Also, anything that is valid as a price is valid as a hire fee, and even items not valid as a price (like specific objects) are valid as hire fees. Second, a question from al-Tatarkhaniyya about custom in currency: if the local custom is to treat five-sixths of a dinar as a dinar, then the contract is interpreted according to that custom, not the literal weight. This is a key principle of urf (custom). Third, a ruling about a broker (dallal): if the broker collects the price and then the currency becomes worthless (kasada), he is not liable if he sold with the owner's permission. Additionally, if a buyer mistakenly pays extra, and the seller takes it and it is lost, the seller is not liable for the excess because he took it as part of the price, but he must return it if demanded? The text cuts off at the page, but the ruling is clear that the seller is not liable for loss of the extra amount, as he did not have ownership of it? Actually, the text says he is considered to have received the price and has no liability for the extra because he only received his due (a thousand) and the excess is not his, but if it is lost from his hand, he may be liable? The passage states 'wa la daman 'alayhi' (no liability) because he fulfilled his right and the excess may be considered a trust. This is a nuanced point.

Key Terms
كيل (kayl): Measurement by volume (for dry goods).
عرف (urf): Custom; in this context, the prevailing practice regarding currency conversion.
دلال (dallal): Broker or agent who mediates the sale and collects the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, the buyer must pay again
B) No, there is no claim against the buyer if the broker sold with the owner's permission
C) The broker must compensate
D) Both the buyer and broker share the loss

Key Takeaway
When the price is a measurable debt, specify the delivery place; custom determines the real value; and a broker is not liable for loss after collecting with permission.

Seven Cases Involving Dinars as Price
Listing seven scenarios where dinars appear as price in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and distinguish the seven Hanafi scenarios where dinars appear as the price in a sale, and identify the ruling in each case.

Core Idea
Seven specific sale cases where currency type between dinar and dirham determines validity or voidness.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents seven canonical scenarios from Hanafi fiqh where the currency of payment (dinar vs. dirham) changes the ruling. These are memorization anchors for muftis and judges, highlighting key principles in sale contracts, mudaraba, preemption, and coercion. Understanding these scenarios helps avoid common pitfalls in transactions involving different currencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scenario and its core transaction
Read the seven cases one by one. Note the parties involved (judge, mudarib, seller, buyer, preemption partner, coerced person) and the transaction type (sale, exchange, preemption, coercion).
Each case has a distinct factual pattern.
2
Determine the ruling (valid/invalid/void)
For each scenario, decide whether the action is permitted, void, or subject to conditions. The text uses phrases like 'يصح', 'لا يجوز', 'بطلت', or 'يصير مكرهاً'.
Note the legal consequence of each.
3
Note the key condition or rationale
Identify the condition that triggers the ruling (e.g., timing of payment, exchange before price payment, equivalence of value, consent under coercion). Understand the rationale: preventing riba, protecting partners' rights, ensuring free consent.
Conditions are often about timing or equivalence.
4
Link to broader fiqh principles
Connect each scenario to broader Hanafi principles such as: currency riba (sarf), mudaraba conditions, preemption (shuf'a) requirements, and coercion (ikrah) in contracts.
This helps internalize the principles behind the rulings.
5
Memorize the list as an anchor
The text is a memorization anchor. Commit the seven cases to memory, as they are frequently cited in fiqh discussions on currency-related sales.
The author clarifies it is not exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدنانير في سبعة مواضع: الأولى بيع القاضي دنانيره لقضاء دينه الدراهم وعكسه. الثانية يصرفها المضارب إذا مات رب المال أو عزل لتصير كرأس المال الثالثة لو كان رأس المال في يد المضارب دراهم فاشترى بدنانير كان للمضارب الرابعة باعه بدراهم ثم اشتراه قبل النقد بدنانير أقل قيمة لم يجز الخامسة لو شراه بدراهم فباعه بربح ثم شراه بدنانير لا يرابح . السادسة أخبر الشفيع أنه شراه بألف درهم فسلم ثم ظهر أن البيع بدنانير أقل قيمة أو أكثر بطلت. السابعة أكره على البيع بدراهم فباع بدنانير مساوية يصير مكرهاً اهـ مختصراً ليس للحصر.
The dinars in seven places: First: the judge selling his dinars to pay his debt of dirhams and vice versa. Second: the mudarib exchanges them when the owner dies or is dismissed to make them like capital. Third: if the capital in the mudarib's hand was dirhams, then he bought with dinars, it is for the mudarib. Fourth: he sold it with dirhams, then bought it before payment with dinars of lesser value, it is not permissible. Fifth: if he bought it with dirhams, sold it at a profit, then bought it with dinars, there is no profit calculation. Sixth: the preemption partner informed that he bought it for a thousand dirhams and he relinquished, then it appeared that the sale was with dinars of lesser or greater value, the preemption is void. Seventh: he was compelled to sell with dirhams but sold with equivalent dinars, he becomes coerced. End, summarized, not for enumeration.

Each scenario is self-explanatory within the Hanafi legal framework. The author notes that this is not an exhaustive list but a selection of key applications.

Key Terms
دينار (dinar): A gold coin used as currency in classical Islamic law.
ثمن (thaman): The price or consideration in a sale contract.

Rules for Measured and Weighed Items as Price
Intra-Hanafi dispute on price specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the invalidity of a sale when a measured/weighed price as a specific item becomes unavailable and the validity when it is a liability, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on specifying the place of fulfillment.

Core Idea
A measured/weighed price as a liability survives unavailability but requires place specification according to Abu Hanifa.

This lesson unpacks two related rulings from the Hanafi tradition on using measurable or weighable items as the price in a sale. The first ruling addresses whether the sale becomes invalid if the item goes out of season or becomes unavailable; the answer depends on whether the price was a specific item or a liability. The second ruling reveals an intra-madhhab disagreement on whether the place of delivery must be explicitly stated when the price is a liability. By understanding these distinctions, you will navigate common pitfalls in commodity-based pricing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for a kaili/wazni price
Distinguish between making a specific kaili/wazni item the price (e.g., 'I sell this slave for these grapes') and making it a liability in the seller's obligation (e.g., 'for a load of wheat on my responsibility'). The ruling on discontinuity differs.
The text first quotes a general rule, then refines it.
2
Apply the ruling on discontinuity
If the price is a specific item (e.g., grapes) and it becomes unavailable, the sale is invalid. However, if the price is a liability (e.g., a debt of fresh dates), the sale is not invalidated by the end of the season; the buyer may wait for the next season.
The text corrects a common misunderstanding.
3
Recognize the dispute on place specification
When the price is a liability (measured/weighed), Abu Hanifa requires specifying the place of fulfillment; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad deem the contract location sufficient. Identify each position and their implied evidence.
The text declares Abu Hanifa's view as 'al-sahih'.
4
Understand the thamarah (practical consequence)
If the place is not specified according to Abu Hanifa, the sale may be fasid (defective) or subject to dispute. This affects the validity of numerous transactions.
The dispute has real implications for contracting parties.
5
Generalize the principle to rent (ujrah)
The text concludes that anything valid as price is also valid as rent; even specific objects (a'yan) can be rent, though they cannot be price for a debt.
This shows symmetry in Hanafi contract law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين برقم (قش) لو جعل الكيلي أو الوزني ثمناً بأن جعل العنب مثلاً ثمناً فانقطع يفسد البيع. ثم رقم (ط) قولهم بأنه يفسد بانقطاعه ليس بصحيح فإن من اشترى شيئاً بقفيز رطب في الذمة فانقطع أوانه لا ينتقض البيع، ولو جعل الكيلي أو الوزني الذمة يشترط بيان محل الإيفاء حتى لو باع قناً بكر برّ في الذمة فإنه يشترط بيان محل إيفائه عند أبي حنيفة وهو الصحيح، وعندهما يتعين محل العقد للإيفاء وما يصلح ثمناً يصلح أجرة، وما لا يصلح ثمناً يصلح أجرة أيضاً كالأعيان اهـ.
And in Jami' al-Fusulain, number (qash): if one makes a measured or weighed item as price, like making grapes as price, and it becomes unavailable, the sale becomes invalid. Then number (tah): their statement that it becomes invalid by its discontinuation is not correct; for whoever buys something with a qafiz of fresh dates as a liability, and its season ends, the sale is not rescinded. And if one makes a measured or weighed item as a liability, specification of the place of fulfillment is required, so if one sold a slave for a load of wheat as a liability, then specification of the place of fulfillment is required according to Abu Hanifa, and that is correct. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the place of the contract is determined for fulfillment. And what is suitable as price is suitable as rent, and what is not suitable as price is also suitable as rent, like specific objects. End.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale where a measurable or weighable item (like grapes or wheat) is made the price. The initial ruling from Jami' al-Fusulain states that if such an item becomes unavailable (e.g., out of season), the sale is invalid. However, the author then cites a correction: if the item is a liability in the seller's obligation (like a debt of fresh dates), the sale is not invalidated by the end of the season because the buyer can wait for the next season. The second point discusses the requirement to specify the place of delivery when the price is a liability: Abu Hanifa insists on specifying the place, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the contract location suffices. The text concludes with a general principle: anything that can be valid price can also be valid as rental payment (ujrah), and even things that cannot be price (like specific objects) can be valid as rent.

Key Terms
كيلي (kaili): An item measured by volume (such as grains, dates).
وزني (wazni): An item weighed (such as gold, silver, wheat by weight).
الذمة (al-dhimmah): Liability or obligation; here meaning the price is a debt obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The buyer may wait for next season
C) The seller must pay the value

Key Takeaway
The invalidity of a sale due to unavailability of a measured/weighed price depends on whether the price is a specific item or a liability; additionally, specifying the place of delivery is required by the preferred Hanafi view.
