Custom Determines the Currency in Sale
Fatwa on custom as interpretive guide in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that custom (urf) determines the meaning of ambiguous terms in sale contracts and evaluate when custom overrides literal interpretation.

Core Idea
Custom clarifies ambiguity: in sales, what people customarily use as currency becomes the contract's intent.

The Hanafi school recognizes custom (urf) as a binding source of law in commercial transactions. This fatwa from al-Tatarhaniyya illustrates a classic scenario: a sale is contracted for 'ten dinars,' but local usage treats a lesser amount as the de facto unit. The question is whether the literal weight or the customary value applies. The answer shapes how we understand contractual obligations in real markets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual ambiguity
The contract mentions 'dinars' but the local custom uses a different unit (five-sixths of a full dinar). This creates uncertainty about the intended price.
Ambiguity arises when the term has both a literal meaning and a customary meaning.
2
Determine the authority of custom
Hanafi law holds that well-known custom (al-'urf al-sahih) is a valid source for interpreting contracts. The fatwa cites that the custom was 'established' and 'well-known' (ishtaharat).
Custom must be general and consistent to override literal meaning.
3
Apply the principle of dalalah al-urf
Customary practice is treated as an implicit condition (dalalah) that the parties are presumed to accept. Thus, the contract is interpreted according to what people customarily do.
Hanafi maxim: 'Dalalah al-urf is like an explicit stipulation.'
4
State the ruling
The seller cannot demand full dinar weight; the contract is valid for the customary amount (five-sixths). The fatwa says: 'It is interpreted according to what people customarily do among themselves.'
The hukm level is wajib: the contract must be executed as per custom.
5
Note the limitation
Custom only clarifies ambiguity; it cannot override an explicit, clear stipulation. If parties expressly agree on full weight, custom does not apply.
This prevents misuse of custom to contradict explicit terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية معزياً إلى النوازل : سئل والدي عمن باع شيئاً من آخر بعشرة دنانير وقد استقرت العادة في ذلك البلد أنهم يصرفون الأثمان فيما بينهم فيعطون كل خمسة أسداس مكان الدينار واشتهرت تلك العادة هل لبائع ذلك العين أن يطالب المشتري بالوزن أم ينعقد العقد على الذي تعارفه المسلمون فيما بينهم بطريق الدلالة ؟ فقال : ينصرف إلى ما تعارفه الناس فيما بينهم اهـ.
And in al-Tatarhaniyya, attributed to al-Nawazil: My father was asked: regarding a man who sold something to another for ten dinars, and the custom in that town was established that they exchange prices among themselves, giving five-sixths for a dinar, and that custom became well-known. Does the seller have the right to demand from the buyer full weight (of the dinar), or is the contract concluded based on what Muslims customarily do among themselves by way of indication? He said: It is interpreted according to what people customarily do among themselves. End.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This fatwa from al-Tatarhaniyya addresses a practical issue: when a sale contract mentions 'dinars' but local custom uses a different currency (like five-sixths of a full dinar as the standard unit). The question is whether the seller can insist on the literal weight of a dinar or whether the contract is interpreted according to custom. The answer is that the contract is interpreted according to the custom (urf) of the people in that town. This is based on the legal principle that custom is authoritative in commercial transactions. The fatwa shows that Hanafi law accommodates local usage in determining the meaning of ambiguous terms.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Customary practice that determines the meaning of ambiguous terms in contracts.
دلالة (dalalah): Indication or implication, here referring to custom as an implied condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fatwa, if the custom is to give five-sixths of a full dinar, what is the seller entitled to?
A) Full dinar weight
B) The customary five-sixths
C) The value in silver dirhams
D) Nothing, as the sale is invalid

Key Takeaway
Custom clarifies contractual ambiguity in Hanafi law; a well-known custom (urf) determines the intended meaning of terms in sales.
